Pre-Islamic Makkan Beliefs: Polytheism and Dahr
Nasafi describes the idolatry and materialist beliefs of Makkans before Islam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that pre-Islamic Makkans held two distinct false beliefs: polytheism (worshipping carved idols, attributing partners and children to Allah) and the belief in the eternity of time (dahr), attributing all events to it.

Core Idea
Pre-Islamic Makkans were polytheists and materialists.

In this passage, Nasafi describes the religious landscape of Makkah before Islam. He clarifies that the protection of the Kaaba from the elephant army was not due to the Makkans' righteousness, because they were engaged in polytheism and some even held materialist views. This sets the stage for the coming of the Prophet.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, then read the literal translation. Notice the Quranic verses embedded in the text.
The passage quotes Quran 6:100 and 45:24.
2
Identify the two false beliefs
The passage mentions two distinct beliefs: polytheism (شرك) and belief in the eternity of time (دهر). Locate where each is described.
Polytheism includes worshipping carved idols and attributing partners to Allah. The dahr belief is expressed by the statement 'nothing destroys us except time.'
3
Reflect on the Quranic support
Nasafi uses Quranic verses to illustrate these beliefs. Think about why he quotes these specific verses and how they refute the false beliefs.
Verse 6:100 condemns associating partners with Allah; verse 45:24 refutes the idea that time is the sole cause of death.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم مما لا يرتاب ذلك لم يكن ممن كان بها يومئذ من الناس لاجل أنفسهم إذ هم كانوا مشركين يعبدون ما ينحتون وَجَعَلُوا لِلَّهِ شُرَكَاءَ الْجِنَّ وَخَلَقَهُمْ وَخَرَقُوا لَهُ بَنِينَ وَبَنَاتِ بِغَيْرِ عِلْمٍ سُبْحَنَهُ وَتَعَالَى عَمَّا يَصِفُونَ، وبعضهم كانوا قائلين بقدم الدهر ويضيفون الحوادث إليه وكانوا يقولون: وَمَا يُهْلِكُنا إِلَّا الدَّهْرُ
Then, what is not doubted is that those who were there at that time were not (punished) for their own sakes, for they were polytheists, worshipping what they carved, and they made for Allah partners of the jinn while He created them, and they falsely attributed to Him sons and daughters without knowledge. Glory be to Him and exalted above what they describe. And some of them were sayers of the eternity of time (dahr), attributing events to it, and they used to say: 'And nothing destroys us except time (al-dahr).'
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is a description of the pre-Islamic Makkans’ beliefs. Nasafi clarifies that the destruction of the elephant army was not due to the Makkans’ own righteousness, because they were polytheists. He quotes Quranic verses (6:100 and 45:24) to illustrate their two major false beliefs: first, they associated partners with Allah, such as from the jinn, and attributed children to Him. Second, some of them believed in the eternity of time (dahr), attributing all events to it rather than to Allah. This shows that the Makkans were not all simply idol worshippers; some held a materialist view of the world. Nasafi's point is that the protection of Makkah was not due to their worthiness but was an Ẓ. prelude to the Prophet's mission.

Key Terms
شرك
دهر
قديم
إشراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All pre-Islamic Arabs were simple idolaters who only worshipped statues.
Correction: Nasafi shows that some held a materialist view, attributing all events to time, not to Allah.

Checkpoint
What two types of false beliefs does Nasafi attribute to the pre-Islamic Makkans?
A) Atheism and polytheism
B) Polytheism and belief in a creator
C) Polytheism and belief in the eternity of time
D) Idolatry and monotheism

Key Takeaway
Nasafi identifies polytheism and the belief in the eternity of time as the dominant false beliefs of pre-Islamic Makkans.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own life, do you ever unconsciously behave as if events 'just happen' or are governed by impersonal forces like time or chance, rather than seeing Allah’s hand in every moment? Can you identify one specific situation where you acted as if Allah was not in control, and what can you do today to replace that with trust in His decree?
?
The pre-Islamic Arabs attributed sons and daughters to Allah without knowledge. Do you ever attribute to Allah qualities or make claims about Him that are not based on authentic revelation? Search your heart: is there any subtle 'saying about Allah' in your speech that mirrors their ignorance, and how will you rectify it?
?
These people worshipped idols they carved with their own hands—an obvious form of shirk. Yet, what 'idols' have you set up in your life that compete with your devotion to Allah? A career, a relationship, your reputation? Today, take a concrete step to dethrone one such idol and realign your heart solely to Allah. What will that step be?

Belief in the Revealed Books
The author briefly asserts belief in the revealed books and names them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic belief in the revealed books and the specific scriptures named in the Qur'an.

Core Idea
Belief in the revealed books is an article of faith, and the Qur'an explicitly names four earlier scriptures.

In this passage, the author confirms that belief in the divinely revealed books is a core Islamic doctrine. He reminds us that the Qur'an itself mentions the scrolls, Torah, Gospel, and Psalms by name, establishing their authenticity. This lesson will help you appreciate the continuity of revelation across prophets.

Guided Path
1
Read the original Arabic
Read the Arabic passage silently, focusing on the words for the books. Then compare with the translation.
Notice the author's concise style.
2
Identify the four scriptures
Find the four names mentioned: الصُّحُف (al-Ṣuḥuf), التَّوْرَاة (al-Tawrāh), الْإِنْجِيل (al-Injīl), الزَّبُور (al-Zabūr).
These correspond to the scrolls of Abraham and Moses, the Torah, the Gospel, and the Psalms.
3
Connect to the proof
The author says belief in the books is established by two verses (from earlier). Consider how the Qur'an itself attests to these scriptures.
The verses likely include 2:285 and 4:136.
4
Reflect on the significance
Why does the author explicitly name these books? It shows continuity of revelation and affirms the truth of earlier prophets.
This counters any notion that Islam rejects earlier scriptures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا الْإِيمَانُ بِالْكُتُبِ فَقَدْ وَرَدَ بِهِ لَهُ عَلَى مَا ذَكَرْنَا مِنَ الْآيَتَيْنِ، وَقَدْ أَفْصَحَ بِذِكْرِ الصُّحُفِ وَذِكْرِ التَّوْرَاةِ وَالْإِنْجِيلِ وَالزَّبُورِ عَلَى مَا لَا يَخْفَى.
As for belief in the books, it has been reported concerning him [the Prophet] based on what we mentioned of the two verses, and he has explicitly mentioned the scrolls and mentioned the Torah, the Gospel, and the Psalms, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is a brief affirmation of the Islamic belief in the divine books. The author states that the obligation to believe in the books is established by the two verses previously cited (from Surah al-Baqarah and al-Nisa'). He then notes that the Qur'an explicitly names the earlier scriptures: the Ṣuḥuf (scrolls of Abraham and Moses), the Tawrāh (Torah), the Injīl (Gospel), and the Zabūr (Psalms). The phrase 'as is not hidden' indicates that these names are well-known and uncontroversial among Muslims. The passage simply confirms this article of faith without further elaboration.

Key Terms

الصحف
التوراة
الإنجيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The Torah and the Gospel only
B) The scrolls, the Torah, the Gospel, and the Psalms
C) The Psalms and the Gospel only
D) The Torah, the Gospel, and the Qur'an

Key Takeaway
The Qur'an explicitly confirms the earlier scriptures of the scrolls, Torah, Gospel, and Psalms, and belief in them is an integral part of Islamic faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Qur'an, do you consciously reflect that it is the final chapter in a long chain of divine revelations? How would your attentiveness change if you truly believed that every word is part of that legacy?
?
Do you ever view followers of earlier scriptures with superiority, forgetting that their original books were from Allah? What step will you take today to respect their faith while still upholding the truth of Islam?
?
The author states that belief in these books is 'not hidden'—a clear obligation. Is there any area where you have been negligent in fulfilling this article of faith, perhaps by not teaching it to your children or not deepening your own understanding? What concrete action will you take this week to rectify that?

Belief in the Messengers: The Middle Path Between Extremes
The author explains the balanced Islamic belief in messengers and contrasts it with Jewish and Christian extremes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Islam preserves a balanced belief in the messengers, avoiding both exaggeration (ghuluw) and denial (taqsir), as exemplified by the Quranic description of prophets as sinless chosen servants.

Core Idea
True faith in prophets is neither deifying them nor denying their rank.

Belief in the messengers of God is a fundamental pillar of faith. But what is the correct stance? The Quran and the Islamic tradition provide a clear middle path. In this passage, the author explains how God preserved the Muslim community from two dangerous extremes: elevating prophets beyond their human status or diminishing their role. Through examples from earlier communities, we see the consequences of both errors and the balanced Islamic position.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Two Extremes
Read the passage and identify the two extremes mentioned: ghuluw (exaggeration) and taqsir (diminishment). The author states that God protected the Muslim community from both.
2
Grasp the Quranic Description
Notice the Quranic description of prophets as 'chosen servants, excellent, protected (ma'sumun)'. This forms the foundation of the balanced view.
This description affirms their high status while clearly stating they are servants, not divine.
3
Analyze the Examples
The author gives examples from the People of the Book. Identify which examples illustrate taqsir (Jews' treatment of Lot and Abraham) and which illustrate ghuluw (Christians' claim about Jesus and Jews' claim about Ezra).
This helps internalize the concept by contrasting it with real historical errors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَکَذَا الْإِيمَانُ بِالرُّسُلِ وَرَدَ بِهِ وَكُرِّرَ ذِكْرُهُمْ فِي مَوَاضِعَ مِنْ كِتَابِهِ، وَصَانَ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى عَقَائِدَ أَهْلِ مِلَّتِهِ عَنِ الْغُلُوِّ فِيهِمْ وَالتَّقْصِيرِ فَقَالَ: إِنَّهُمْ عِبَادٌ مُصْطَفَوْنَ وَخِيَارٌ مَعْصُومُونَ، فَمَا نَسَبُوا أَحَدًا مِنْهُمْ إِلَى الْفُجُورِ كَنِسْبَةِ الْيَهُودِ لُوطًا إِلَى الْفُجُورِ بِابْنَتِهِ فِي حَالِ السُّكْرِ، وَلَا جَحَدُوا أَحَدًا مِنْهُمْ كَجُحُودِ الْيَهُودِ نُبُوَّةَ إِبْرَاهِيمَ عَلَيْهِ السَّلَامُ مَعَ جَلَالِ قَدْرِهِ وَرُتْبَتِهِ فِي الْأَنْبِيَاءِ عَلَيْهِمُ السَّلَامُ، وَاقْتِصَارِهِمْ عَلَى الْقَوْلِ أَنَّهُ كَانَ رَجُلًا صَالِحًا، وَلَا ادَّعَوْا فِي أَحَدٍ مِنْهُمْ مَا ادَّعَتْهُ النَّصَارَى فِي عِيسَى وَالْيَهُودُ فِي عُزَيْرٍ عَلَيْهِمَا السَّلَامُ.
And similarly, belief in the messengers has been reported and their mention is repeated in places of His Book. And God Most High preserved the beliefs of the people of His community from extremism (ghuluw) concerning them and from deficiency (taqsir). He said: They are chosen servants and excellent ones, protected (ma‘ṣumūn). So they did not attribute any of them to immorality, like the attribution of the Jews of Lot to immorality with his daughter in a state of drunkenness, nor did they deny any of them like the denial of the Jews of the prophethood of Abraham, peace be upon him, despite the majesty of his rank and station among the prophets, peace be upon them, and their restricting themselves to saying that he was a righteous man. Nor did they claim concerning any of them what the Christians claimed concerning Jesus and the Jews concerning Ezra, peace be upon them both.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal statement about belief in the messengers. The author first notes that belief in the messengers is commanded and that God preserved the Muslim community from two extremes: ghuluw (exaggeration) and taqsir (diminishment). The Quranic description of prophets as 'chosen servants, excellent, and ma‘ṣūmūn (protected from sin)' sets the correct middle path. The author then illustrates these extremes with examples from the People of the Book. The Jews are cited for two forms of taqsir: (1) attributing immorality to Prophet Lot (accusing him of incest with his daughter while drunk), and (2) denying the prophethood of Abraham, calling him merely a righteous man. The Christians are cited for ghuluw: claiming Jesus is the Son of God. The Jews are also cited for ghuluw in the case of Ezra, whom they allegedly called the Son of God. The Muslim community avoids all these errors by adhering to the Quranic description of prophets as sinless chosen servants.

Key Terms
الإيمان بالرسل
الغلو
التقصير
المعصومون
عباد مصطفون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that prophets can commit major sins or that they are divine beings.
Correction: The Quran describes them as 'chosen servants' and 'protected (ma‘ṣūmūn)', meaning they are sinless but not divine. The middle path is to affirm their high status without exaggeration or denial.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how did the Jews err regarding Prophet Lot?
A) They denied his prophethood
B) They claimed he was the son of God
C) They accused him of immorality with his daughter
D) They said he was merely a righteous man

Key Takeaway
The balanced Islamic view of prophets is that they are sinless chosen servants of God, avoiding both the Jewish/Christian extremes of denial and deification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of the prophets, do you ever find yourself either downplaying their status (like considering them just good people) or elevating them beyond human limits? Which tendency is more tempting for you, and how does this passage call you to correct it?
?
The Jews denied Abraham's prophethood. Are there any teachings of the Prophet Muhammad (PBUH) that you sometimes downplay or ignore? How does the concept of 'taqsir' apply to your own practice?
?
The Christians deified Jesus. In what ways might you be prone to exaggerating the status of religious figures (scholars, saints) in your life? How does this passage guard you against that?

The Dilemma of Power at the Time of Action
Nasafi deduces that denying power at act's time nullifies commands, prohibitions, and divine justice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's dilemma showing that denying human power at the moment of action leads to either nullifying divine commands or attributing foolishness to God.

Core Idea
Denying power at the time of action makes God either unjust or foolish.

Nasafi presents a powerful logical argument against the Mu'tazila position that humans do not have power at the time of the act. He shows that this view forces a choice between two impossible options: either commands become meaningless, or God commands the impossible. This lesson walks through that argument and its theological consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the translation. Let the author's own argument come through before you interpret.
The passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
Find where Nasafi presents the two possibilities: either the agent is not commanded at the time of the act, or he is. Note the consequences of each.
The first horn leads to invalidation of laws, the second to describing God as foolish.
3
Understand the logical proof
Follow Nasafi's reinforcement: if commanded when the act is impossible, it's absurd; if commanded at a time of power for a future act, the text breaks off but the implication is that power must be present at the time of the act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنهم إن لم يجعلوه وقت الفصل مأموراً منهيًا بهذا الفعل فقد أخرجوه عن كونه طاعة ومعصية وفاعله عن كونه مطيعا وعاصيًا، وفيه إبطال الأوامر والنواهي ورفع الشرائع وإزالة الحل والحرمة، وهذا خروج عن الملة ودخول في قول منكري الشرائع وإبطال العداوة والولاية، وفيه وصف الله تعالى بكونه ظالما بتعذيبه على فعل من هذه الأفعال، وإن جعلوه وقت الفعل مأمورًا منهيًا، فقد وصفوا الله تعالى بالسفه، حيث أمر بما كان العبد مضطرًا إليه عاجزا عنه أو نهى عنه. والذي يقرر هذا: أن العبد لو كان مأمورًا بالفعل أو منهيًا عنه وقت وجود والفعل عنه في تلك الحالة مستحيل فكان مأمورًا بما يستحيل وجوده، منهيًا عما يمنع ثبوته، وهذا محال وإن كان مأمورًا وقت القدرة ليفعل بها في الثاني، لم القدرة
Then if they do not make him at the time of the act commanded or prohibited by this act, they have removed it from being obedience or disobedience and its doer from being obedient or disobedient. That implies the invalidation of commands and prohibitions, the removal of the laws, the elimination of lawful and unlawful. This is a departure from the religion and entering into the statement of the deniers of the laws, and the invalidation of enmity and friendship. It also includes describing Allah as unjust by punishing for such acts. And if they make him at the time of the act commanded or prohibited, then they have described Allah as foolish, because He commands what the servant is compelled to and incapable of, or prohibits it. What establishes this: if the servant were commanded to do an act or prohibited from it at the time of existence, and the act from him in that case is impossible, then he would be commanded to do what is impossible, or prohibited from what cannot be avoided—this is absurd. And if he were commanded at the time of power to do it in the second [moment]? ... power
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage draws the theological consequences of denying power at the time of the act. Nasafi presents a dilemma: either the Mu'tazila do not consider the agent commanded or prohibited at the moment of the act, in which case the act cannot be called obedience or disobedience, leading to the nullification of all religious commands, laws, and even divine justice (God would be unjust to punish). Or they do consider him commanded/prohibited at that moment, which attributes foolishness to God because He commands what the servant is compelled to or incapable of. In either case, the Mu'tazila position leads to absurdity and contradiction with basic Islamic tenets. Nasafi then reinforces this with a logical proof: if the servant is commanded at the time when the act is impossible, he is commanded to do the impossible; if he is commanded at a time when he has power (before the act), then the command effectively refers to a future moment. The final sentence is cut off but likely reiterates that power must be present at the moment of the act for the command to be meaningful.

Key Terms
أمر
نهي
طاعة
معصية
شريعة
ظلم
سفه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Commands can be binding even if the agent lacks ability to perform them at the moment they are commanded.
Correction: Nasafi shows that this would either make God unjust (if no command at act time) or foolish (if command at act time but impossible). Moral responsibility requires power at the time of action.

Checkpoint
What is one consequence Nasafi mentions if the servant is not considered commanded at the time of the act?
A) The act becomes meritorious.
B) It leads to describing God as foolish.
C) It invalidates commands, prohibitions, and laws.
D) The act becomes voluntary.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi's dilemma shows that meaningful moral responsibility requires that the agent have power at the moment of action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever excused your own sin by telling yourself 'I couldn't help it'? Does this passage force you to reconsider that excuse? What will you say to yourself the next time you feel compelled to disobey?
?
If you truly believe God is just and wise, how does the dilemma in this passage reshape your understanding of your own responsibility in moments of temptation? What concrete change will you make in your next moment of weakness?
?
When you hear someone say 'God won't hold me accountable for what I cannot control,' does this passage comfort you or confront you? Which horn of the dilemma applies to that statement, and what does it imply about God's character?

Refuting Abu Hashim: Punishment Without an Act
Objection and refutation of Abu Hashim's claim on punishment without act

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Maturidi tradition refutes Abu Hashim's claim that a person can deserve punishment without committing any act.

Core Idea
Leaving a commanded act is itself an act; no punishment without an act.

This passage presents a powerful objection to Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i's Mu'tazili view that a person can be disobedient and deserve punishment without performing any act. The refutation exposes the logical flaw: not doing a commanded act is itself an act of leaving (tark), which is forbidden. The objection then shows that if a person is truly devoid of both the commanded act and its leaving, he cannot be considered disobedient. This leads to an absurd consequence that contradicts the consensus of the umma.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Recognize that Abu Hashim's view is presented as a corrupt doctrine. The refutation begins by posing a hypothetical scenario.
This is a refutation of a Mu'tazili position.
2
Grasp the hypothetical scenario
Consider a capable person who, for his entire life, neither performs the commanded act nor refrains from the prohibited act. According to Abu Hashim, such a person is still disobedient and deserves punishment, not for an act, but for not doing what was commanded.
Abu Hashim says: 'He would be a disobedient person deserving of blame and punishment, not for an act.'
3
Follow the refutation's logic
The refutation argues that if a person does not perform a commanded act, he has left it (tark), and leaving a commanded act is itself a forbidden act. Therefore, punishment is for that act of leaving. But the hypothetical is described as being devoid of both the commanded act and its leaving, so he cannot be disobedient.
The key insight: 'The one commanded to do an act, if he does not do it, has left it.'
4
See the devastating conclusion
The refutation points out that Abu Hashim's view leads to the claim that Allah would eternally punish someone for no act at all, which contradicts the consensus of the Muslim community. It also uses a tu quoque: Abu Hashim's own predecessors declared Ahl al-Sunna disbelievers for saying punishment is for a created act, but here he goes further.
The passage ends with a rhetorical punch: 'So it is more fitting to declare disbelief [against you].'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن أبا هاشم كما أحدث هذا المذهب الفاسد، قيل له: أرأيت لو كان هذا القادر الخالي عن الفعل إيجابًا وتركّاً مدة عمره لو كان مأمورًا بفعل منهيًا عن فعل آخر ماذا يكون حاله ؟ فقال : يكون عاصيًا مستحقاً للذم والعقاب لا على فعل لأنه لم يفعل ما أمر به مع قدرته عليه وتوفر آلاته وارتفاع الموانع عنه. فقيل له: كيف صار مستحقاً للعقاب بأن لم يفعل ما أمر به، والمأمور بفعل إذا لم يفعله فقد تركه، وترك المأمور به حرام فاستحق العقاب على الفعل وهو ترك المأمور به إذ تركه فعل منهي عنه قبيح فقد ارتكبه التارك، فهذا إذا خلا عن الفعل المأمور به وعن تركه لم يصر مطيعا بفعله ولا عاصيًا بارتكاب تركه المنهي عنه فكيف صار مستحقاً للعقاب بل للخلود لا على فعل؟ وهل صار مطيعا مستحقاً للمدح والثواب بأن لم يفعل ما نهى عنه وهو الترك؟ وهل هذا منك إلا القول بأن الله تعالى يخلد أحدًا في النار لا على فعل حصل منه، والأمة أجمعت على خلاف هذا، وسلفك يكفرون أهل السنة بقولهم باستحقاق العقاب على فعل خلقه الله تعالى، وإن كانوا يقولون ذلك بفعل العبد وكسبه وهو به عاص، فكان إكفار أولى إذ زعمت أن الله تعالى يعذب العبد لا على فعل محدث أو مكتسب.
Then, when Abu Hashim had originated this corrupt doctrine, it was said to him: 'Consider if this capable person, devoid of both doing and leaving an act for his entire life, were commanded to do an act and prohibited from another act, what would his state be?' He said: 'He would be a disobedient person deserving of blame and punishment, not for an act, because he did not do what he was commanded, despite his ability, availability of means, and absence of obstacles.' It was said to him: 'How did he become deserving of punishment by not doing what he was commanded? The one commanded to do an act, if he does not do it, has left it. Leaving what is commanded is forbidden, so he deserves punishment for the act, namely leaving what is commanded, because his leaving it is a forbidden ugly act that the leaver commits. So if he is devoid of both the commanded act and its leaving, he does not become obedient by his act nor disobedient by committing its forbidden leaving. So how did he become deserving of punishment, even eternal punishment, not for an act? And would he become obedient deserving of praise and reward by not doing what was prohibited, which is the leaving? And is this from you except the statement that Allah the Exalted eternally punishes someone in the Fire not for an act that occurred from him? And the community (al-umma) has unanimously disagreed with this, and your predecessors declare the Ahl al-Sunna disbelievers for their statement of deserving punishment for an act created by Allah the Exalted, even though they say that by the servant's act and acquisition (kasb) and he is thereby disobedient. So it is more fitting to declare disbelief [against you] since you claim that Allah the Exalted torments the servant not for an originated or acquired act.'
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer refutation of Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i's Mu'tazili view. The objection poses a hypothetical: a person who is capable, has no obstacles, but neither performs the commanded act nor commits the prohibited act for his entire life. According to Abu Hashim, such a person would still be disobedient and deserve punishment, not for any act, but simply for not doing what was commanded. The refutation then points out the inconsistency: if a person is commanded to do something and does not do it, he has indeed performed an act — the act of leaving (tark). Leaving a commanded act is forbidden and ugly, so it qualifies as an act for which punishment is deserved. But the hypothetical person is described as being devoid of both the commanded act and its leaving; meaning he does not even commit the act of leaving. In that case, he cannot be considered disobedient or deserving punishment. The refutation then draws a devastating conclusion: Abu Hashim's view implies that Allah would eternally punish someone for no act at all, which contradicts the consensus of the Muslim community (umma). Furthermore, the refutation uses a tu quoque argument: Abu Hashim's own predecessors (Mu'tazila) accuse the Ahl al-Sunna of disbelief for saying that punishment can be for an act created by Allah, even though they attribute it to the servant's acquisition (kasb). But here Abu Hashim goes further by claiming punishment for no act at all, so he is more deserving of being declared a disbeliever.

Key Terms
عاصي
استحقاق العقاب
ترك المأمور به
خلود
كسب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A person can be punished for doing nothing at all.
Correction: Not doing a commanded act is itself an act (leaving), which is forbidden. But if a person is truly devoid of both the act and its leaving, he cannot be considered disobedient.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, what act does a person commit when he does not perform a commanded act?
A) He commits no act at all
B) He commits the act of leaving (tark)
C) He commits an act of obedience
D) He commits a neutral act

Key Takeaway
Abu Hashim's claim is invalid because leaving a commanded act is itself an act, and punishment without any act contradicts the consensus of the community.

?

Two Absurd Consequences of Abu Hashim's View
Two absurd consequences of Abu Hashim's view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two absurd consequences that follow from Abu Hashim's theory of good and evil: double punishment for omission-and-commission and the conclusion that leaving prayer is good.

Core Idea
Abu Hashim's view leads to heretical conclusions: double punishment and calling neglect of prayer good.

In this passage, the author continues his refutation of Abu Hashim by showing that his position leads to two unacceptable consequences. First, if omitting a commanded good act merits punishment, then a person who both omits the command and commits a sin deserves double punishment. Second, if not-prohibited actions are considered good, then leaving prayer—which is not explicitly prohibited in his view—becomes good, which is clearly against Islam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the two distinct objections introduced by 'ثم قيل له'.
2
Identify the first objection
Find the objection about double punishment. Abu Hashim says a person who omits a command and changes in an ugly way deserves two portions of punishment.
The objection extends to the case of adultery: two hadd punishments.
3
Identify the second objection
Find the objection about leaving prayer. Abu Hashim's principle that every permissible act is good leads to the conclusion that leaving prayer is good.
This is marked as a complete departure from religion.
4
Reflect on the author's conclusion
Notice how the author condemns both consequences as 'خروج عن أقاويل أهل الإسلام' and 'خروج عن الدين'.
The passage ends with a prayer for protection from such a statement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قيل له: إن هذا المأمور لو لم يفعل المأمورية وتغير تغيرا قبيحا؟ قال: استحق قسطين من العذاب أحدهما للقبيح الذي فعله، والآخر للحسن الذي لم يفعله وهذا كله خروج عن أقاويل أهل الإسلام. ثم قيل له : إن المكلف لما نهى عن الزنا فقد أمر بتركه لا محالة، فينبغي أن يقال : إنه لما زنى يجب عليه حدان أحدهما للزنا الذي فعله، والآخر للحسن الذي لم يفعله وهو ترك الزنا. وكذا في جميع هذه الأفعال، فأما ترك هذه الأفعال فغير واجب عليه، ويلزم ألا يكون ترك الزنا وشرب الخمر واللواطة طاعة ويرتكب ذلك. أسباب الحدود، فزعم عنه توجه هذا الإلزام عليه أنه نهى عن ... وكذا زعم أن ترك الصلاة إذا لم يكن منهيًا عنه ليس بقبيح، ثم عنده ما ليس بقبيح بل هو مباح يكون حسنًا إذ من مذهبه أن كل مباح حسن، وإنما يجب بحسن زائد فيكون على هذا ترك الصلاة حسنًا، وهذا خروج عن الدين والانسلاخ منه بمرة، نعوذ بالله من قول هذا عقباه.
Then it was said to him: If this commanded person did not do the commanded thing and changed with an ugly change? He said: He deserves two portions of punishment: one for the ugly thing he did, and the other for the good thing he did not do. All of this is departure from the sayings of the people of Islam. Then it was said to him: When the morally responsible person (al-mukallaf) is prohibited from adultery, he is commanded to leave it without exception. So it should be said: When he commits adultery, two prescribed punishments (ḥadd) are incumbent on him: one for the adultery he committed, and the other for the good thing he did not do, namely leaving adultery. And likewise in all such acts. As for leaving these acts, it is not incumbent on him, and it follows that leaving adultery, drinking wine, and sodomy is not an act of obedience and he commits that. The causes of the prescribed punishments... So he claimed that the force of this objection applied to him that he prohibited from... And likewise he claimed that leaving prayer, if it is not prohibited, is not ugly. Then, according to him, what is not ugly but permissible is good, since his doctrine is that every permissible thing is good, and it is only obligatory due to an additional goodness. So according to this, leaving prayer is good. And this is departure from the religion and shedding it completely. We seek refuge in Allah from the consequence of this statement.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage contains two further objections to Abu Hashim's position, exposing absurd consequences. The first objection builds on the earlier hypothetical: if the person not only omits the commanded act but also changes with an ugly change (i.e., commits a sin), then Abu Hashim would say he deserves double punishment — one for the ugly act committed and one for the good act (the commanded act) omitted. The refutation dismisses this as a departure from the consensus of Islam. The objection then extends this to a concrete example: if someone is forbidden from adultery, he is by the same token commanded to leave it. If he commits adultery, according to Abu Hashim's logic, he would deserve two hadd punishments — one for the adultery and one for not performing the good act of leaving it. The absurdity is that this would require doubling of penalties for every sin. The text then notes that leaving these sinful acts is not actually obligatory (i.e., one is not commanded to leave them in the sense of a separate command), so the logic fails. The second objection concerns leaving prayer. According to Abu Hashim's classification, if leaving prayer is not explicitly prohibited, it would not be ugly; rather, since every permissible act is good, leaving prayer would be a good act. This leads to the clearly heretical conclusion that neglecting prayer is good, which the author calls a complete shedding of religion. The passage ends with a prayer for protection from such a statement.

Misconception: These objections are merely hypothetical and do not follow necessarily from Abu Hashim's view.
Correction: The author argues that these are necessary logical consequences of Abu Hashim's principles, not just hypothetical possibilities.

Checkpoint

A) Leaving prayer becomes obligatory
B) Leaving prayer becomes ugly
C) Leaving prayer becomes good (ḥasan)
D) Leaving prayer becomes neutral

Key Takeaway
Abu Hashim's view logically leads to two heretical consequences: double punishment for sin-and-omission, and considering the neglect of prayer as good.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The author ends with 'We seek refuge in Allah from the consequence of this statement.' Do you take your theological beliefs seriously enough to examine where they might lead if followed to their logical end? Are you prepared to reject any belief that contradicts the consensus of Ahl al-Sunnah, no matter how appealing it seems?

Critique of a Rationalist View on Leaving Prayer
Critiquing the view that the one who leaves prayer incurs threat due to a disliked action rather than omission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author rejects the view that the threat for leaving prayer is due to performing a disliked action rather than the omission itself.

Core Idea
For Ahl al-Sunna, omission is an act; the threat is for the omission, not a separate disliked action.

This passage engages with a subtle debate within kalam: why does the one who leaves prayer deserve threat? Some rationalists argue that threat applies only if a prohibited act is committed, but the author counters that omission itself is an act according to Ahl al-Sunna. He then criticizes an inconsistent position that tries to combine elements from different schools.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the two views presented: omission as act, and omission as non-act.
2
Identify the two views on omission
The passage presents two accounts: one where the one who leaves prayer commits a prohibited act (omission as act), and another where he simply fails to perform the commanded act (non-existence of the commanded). Note which view belongs to Ahl al-Sunna and which to Abu Hashim.
The author does not explicitly label them but contrasts them.
3
Understand the critique of the inconsistent view
The author criticizes someone who mixes elements: he claims to follow Ahl al-Sunna but holds an unsupported opinion, or follows the Mu'tazila yet disagrees with Abu Hashim by making the command produce a ruling of dislikeness (karahiyya) in the opposite action. See the contradiction.
This is the core of the critique.
4
Reflect on the strangeness of the opinion
The author calls it a strange opinion because if the threat is for the absence of the commanded act, there is no need to posit an additional disliked action. The threat applies regardless.
The author argues that the opinion is rejected by all Ahl al-Qibla.
5
Summarize the author's position
State in your own words why the author holds that omission alone suffices for threat without needing a separate disliked action.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على تارك الصلاة لارتكابه ضدها المنهي عنه وهو الترك الذي هو فعل كما هو أهل القبلة. أما لانعدام ما أمر به من غير فعل ارتكبه كما هو مذهب هاشم. فإن كان متمسكا بمذاهب أهل السنة والجماعة فقال ما قال لرأي منح له غير معروض على قوانين الأصول، ولا مبني على قواعد المذهب، وإن كان مائلاً إلى مذهب القوم في مسألة الكلام وإلى مذهب أبي هاشم في هذه المسألة فقال ما قال بناء على رأيه، غير أنه مع هذا خالف أبا هاشم حيث جعل الأمر حكمًا في ضد المأمور به وهو الكراهية، وأبو هاشم أبى هذا. وهذا من أعجب الرأي؛ فإنه إن كان الوعيد متوجهًا على من انعدم المأمور من قبله وعوقب لذلك كما هو مذهب أبي هاشم، فأي حاجة إلى إثبات الكراهية في الضد، والوعيد بدونه متوجه والعقاب متحقق، وإن لم يكن بد من فعل محظور يرتكبه ووزر عظيم لحقه لتوجه الوعيد وتحقق العقوبة، وذلك هو فعل الترك، فكيف يزعم بتوجهه كل الوعيد (1) الوارد لتارك الفرائض وثبوت العقوبة له لو لم يتغمده الله برحمته بمباشرة فعل مكروه ليس بمنهى عنه ولا محظور، وهذا مما يأباه جميع أهل القبلة.
Regarding the one who leaves prayer because he commits its opposite which is prohibited, namely the omission, which is an act (fi'l) according to Ahl al-Qibla. Or due to the non-existence of what was commanded without committing an act, as is the madhhab of Hashim. If he is adhering to the madhhabs of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a, then what he said is based on an opinion granted to him that is not examined according to the principles of usul, nor built on the rules of the madhhab. And if he is inclined to the madhhab of the people (Mu'tazila) in the matter of speech (kalam) and to the madhhab of Abu Hashim in this issue, then he said what he said based on his opinion, except that he disagreed with Abu Hashim in that he made the command a ruling in the opposite of the commanded, which is disliked (karahiyya), while Abu Hashim rejected this. This is one of the strangest opinions; for if the threat (wa'id) is directed at the one from whom the commanded action is absent and he is punished for that, as is the madhhab of Abu Hashim, then what need is there to affirm dislikeness (karahiyya) in the opposite? The threat is directed without it, and punishment is realized. And if it is necessary that a prohibited act be committed and a great sin incurred for the threat to be directed and punishment realized, and that is the act of omission, then how can he claim that all the threat mentioned for the one who leaves obligations and the punishment established for him, if Allah does not cover him with His mercy, is due to his direct performance of a disliked action that is neither forbidden nor prohibited? And this is rejected by all Ahl al-Qibla.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is a critique of a specific rationalist (likely Mu'tazili) view regarding the one who leaves prayer. The author examines two possible accounts of why the person who abandons prayer deserves punishment: either because he commits a prohibited act (which is the omission itself, considered an act by Ahl al-Qibla), or because he merely fails to perform the commanded act without committing an act (the view of Hashim). The author then criticizes someone who claims to follow Ahl al-Sunna but holds an unsupported opinion, or who follows the Mu'tazila in the issue of kalam and Abu Hashim's view on this issue, yet contradicts Abu Hashim by making the command produce a ruling of dislikeness (karahiyya) in the opposite action. The author finds this contradictory: if the threat is for the absence of the commanded action, then there is no need to posit a disliked action separate from the omission. The threat would apply regardless. The author concludes by saying it is a most strange opinion to claim that the severe threat for abandoning obligations is due to performing a disliked action that is not even forbidden. This, he says, is rejected by all Ahl al-Qibla.

كراهية (karahiyya)
ضد (didd)
ترك (tark)
مكروه (makruh)
محظور (mahzur)
أهل القبلة (Ahl al-Qibla)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Omission is not an act, so threat for leaving prayer requires committing a positive prohibited action.
Correction: According to Ahl al-Sunna, omission is considered an act (fi'l), so the threat for leaving prayer is due to the omission itself, not a separate disliked action.

Checkpoint

B) The view that the threat for leaving prayer is due to performing a disliked action rather than the omission itself.
C) The view that Abu Hashim is correct.
D) The view that Ahl al-Sunna are wrong.

Key Takeaway
The author argues that the threat for leaving prayer is due to the omission itself, not a separate disliked action, and criticizes an inconsistent view that adds unnecessary conditions.

Answering the Objection: Two Senses of Power
Nasafi distinguishes two senses of power to dissolve an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's distinction between instrumental power and the true power of the act, and why the objection about avoiding sin while capable is a category mistake.

Core Idea
The objection confuses two senses of power—one possible, one absurd.

The Mu'tazilites posed a clever objection: if power is simultaneous with the act, then one cannot avoid sin while being capable of it. Nasafi points out that this question mixes up two different meanings of power. Let's see how he untangles them and why his answer is decisive.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice how Nasafi first restates the objection, then offers a conditional answer.
The objection is about avoiding sin while having power (قادر).
2
Identify the two meanings of power
Nasafi distinguishes between power of instruments (قدرة الآلات والأسباب) and true power of the act (حقيقة قدرة الفعل). Understand each one.
Instrumental power is general capability; true power is specific to the act itself.
3
Analyze the logical impossibility
If the objection meant true power of the act, then the question becomes: 'Did anyone avoid sin while doing it?' which is self-contradictory. See why this is impossible.
True power exists only with the act, so they are inseparable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قولهم هل اتقى أحد معصية الله تعالى وهو قادر عليها؟ فنقول: إن عنيت بالقدرة قدرة الآلات والأسباب فنعم، وإن عنيت حقيقة قدرة الفعل كان السؤال محالاً، لأن تلك القدرة توجد مع الفعل فكأنك قلت: هل اتقى أحد معصية الله تعالى وهو فاعل لها وهذا محال. ثم يقابل بمثل هذا التشنيع فيقال: هل أعطى الله تعالى وليا قوة الطاعة بعين الطاعة؟ فإن قال: لا، تمكنت وحشة عظيمة، وإن قال: نعم، أقر بقولنا، فإن قالوا: لا عذر للعبد في الشاهد أعظم من أن يقول: لو قيل له لم لا فعلت كذا؟ فيقول: لأنني لم أقدر عليه، فمثله في الغائب. قلنا: هذا يكون عذرًا فيما منع عنه القدرة لا فيما ضيعها بإيثاره بدله.
As for their saying: 'Did anyone avoid a sin of God while being capable of it?' We say: If you mean by power the power of instruments and causes, then yes; if you mean the true power of the act, then the question is impossible, because that power exists with the act, as if you said: 'Did anyone avoid a sin of God while being a doer of it?' And this is impossible. Then it is countered with a similar objection: 'Did God give a saint the power of obedience exactly in the act of obedience?' If he says no, a great loneliness occurs; if he says yes, he admits our position. And if they say: There is no greater excuse for a servant in the witnessed world than to say, when asked 'Why did you not do such-and-such?', 'Because I was not able to do it', and the like in the unseen. We say: This is an excuse concerning that from which he is prevented from power, not concerning that which he wasted by preferring something else to it.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer (istishkāl wa jawāb) followed by a counter-objection. Nasafi first addresses the Mu'tazilite objection: 'Did anyone ever avoid a sin while being capable of it?' The objection implies that if power is simultaneous with the act, then at the moment of sinning one is capable of it, so avoiding it would require power while not sinning, leading to contradiction. Nasafi answers by distinguishing between two senses of power: instrumental power (power of instruments and causes) and the true power of the act (the power that is specifically for that act). If they mean instrumental power, then yes, one can avoid sin while having that power. If they mean the true power of the act, then the question is absurd because that power exists only with the act itself; asking if one can avoid sin while having that power is like asking if one can avoid sin while doing it—impossible. Nasafi then turns the objection around: he asks the Mu'tazila whether God gives a saint the power of obedience exactly in the act of obedience. If they say no, that leads to a disturbing conclusion. If they say yes, they admit the Maturidi position that power is simultaneous with the act. Finally, he addresses the claim that in the witnessed world, the best excuse is 'I was not able,' implying that in the unseen world, the servant could use the same excuse. Nasafi clarifies that this excuse applies only when one is prevented from power altogether, not when one neglects to use power given by preferring something else.

Key Terms
قدرة الآلات
قدرة الفعل
اتقى
معصية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if power is simultaneous with the act, then avoiding sin is impossible because one is always capable of it.
Correction: Nasafi clarifies that the power simultaneous with the act is the true power of that act, not a general power. One can avoid sin while having general instrumental power.

Checkpoint
What distinction does Nasafi make in answering the objection about avoiding sin while capable?
A) Distinction between divine power and human power.
B) Distinction between power of instruments/causes and true power of the act.
C) Distinction between past and future power.
D) Distinction between potential and actual power.

Key Takeaway
The objection about avoiding sin while capable fails because it confuses two different meanings of power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, have you ever justified it by telling yourself 'I had no choice' or 'I couldn't help it'? In light of Nasafi's distinction between general power and specific power, how honest are you really about your own freedom and responsibility? Will you now commit to stop using that excuse and instead acknowledge that you had the general ability to avoid sin but chose not to?
?
The objector's question tries to trap you into a logical dead end to avoid moral accountability. In your own life, do you use intellectual arguments or theological puzzles to sidestep the plain command of Allah? What would it mean for you to drop those defenses and simply submit to what you know is right, regardless of unanswered questions?
?
Nasafi shows that having the capacity (power of instruments) does not force you to sin. How does this truth confront the moments you have felt 'overwhelmed' by temptation? Will you now pray to Allah for the true power (قدرة الفعل) to accompany your act of obedience, while recognizing that your general ability is already a gift from Him?

The Counter-Objection: Power of Obedience in the Act
Nasafi counters the objection with a parallel question about the power of obedience.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's counter-objection that forces the Mu'tazilites to either admit a disturbing conclusion or accept simultaneous power.

Core Idea
Turning the tables: if God gives power of obedience exactly in the act, the objection collapses.

Nasafi doesn't just answer the objection—he launches a counter-attack. He asks the Mu'tazilites whether God gives a saint the power of obedience exactly in the act of obedience. Their answer will either lead to an unsettling implication or admit the Maturidi position. Let's see how this argument works.

Guided Path
1
Read the counter-objection passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice that Nasafi frames it as a parallel to the previous objection (بمثل هذا التشنيع).
This is a rhetorical move to show consistency.
2
Analyze the two possible answers
The Mu'tazilite can say 'no' or 'yes' to whether God gives the power of obedience exactly in the act. Understand the consequence of each.
If 'no' leads to 'great loneliness'—a disturbing absence of divine support. If 'yes' admits the Maturidi claim that power is simultaneous.
3
Understand why this refutes the original objection
The original objection assumed that power must precede the act. By showing that the Mu'tazilite position leads to problems, Nasafi defends the Maturidi view.
This is a typical kalam technique: argue from the opponent's own premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قولهم هل اتقى أحد معصية الله تعالى وهو قادر عليها؟ فنقول: إن عنيت بالقدرة قدرة الآلات والأسباب فنعم، وإن عنيت حقيقة قدرة الفعل كان السؤال محالاً، لأن تلك القدرة توجد مع الفعل فكأنك قلت: هل اتقى أحد معصية الله تعالى وهو فاعل لها وهذا محال. ثم يقابل بمثل هذا التشنيع فيقال: هل أعطى الله تعالى وليا قوة الطاعة بعين الطاعة؟ فإن قال: لا، تمكنت وحشة عظيمة، وإن قال: نعم، أقر بقولنا، فإن قالوا: لا عذر للعبد في الشاهد أعظم من أن يقول: لو قيل له لم لا فعلت كذا؟ فيقول: لأنني لم أقدر عليه، فمثله في الغائب. قلنا: هذا يكون عذرًا فيما منع عنه القدرة لا فيما ضيعها بإيثاره بدله.
As for their saying: 'Did anyone avoid a sin of God while being capable of it?' We say: If you mean by power the power of instruments and causes, then yes; if you mean the true power of the act, then the question is impossible, because that power exists with the act, as if you said: 'Did anyone avoid a sin of God while being a doer of it?' And this is impossible. Then it is countered with a similar objection: 'Did God give a saint the power of obedience exactly in the act of obedience?' If he says no, a great loneliness occurs; if he says yes, he admits our position. And if they say: There is no greater excuse for a servant in the witnessed world than to say, when asked 'Why did you not do such-and-such?', 'Because I was not able to do it', and the like in the unseen. We say: This is an excuse concerning that from which he is prevented from power, not concerning that which he wasted by preferring something else to it.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer (istishkāl wa jawāb) followed by a counter-objection. Nasafi first addresses the Mu'tazilite objection: 'Did anyone ever avoid a sin while being capable of it?' The objection implies that if power is simultaneous with the act, then at the moment of sinning one is capable of it, so avoiding it would require power while not sinning, leading to contradiction. Nasafi answers by distinguishing between two senses of power: instrumental power (power of instruments and causes) and the true power of the act (the power that is specifically for that act). If they mean instrumental power, then yes, one can avoid sin while having that power. If they mean the true power of the act, then the question is absurd because that power exists only with the act itself; asking if one can avoid sin while having that power is like asking if one can avoid sin while doing it—impossible. Nasafi then turns the objection around: he asks the Mu'tazila whether God gives a saint the power of obedience exactly in the act of obedience. If they say no, that leads to a disturbing conclusion. If they say yes, they admit the Maturidi position that power is simultaneous with the act. Finally, he addresses the claim that in the witnessed world, the best excuse is 'I was not able,' implying that in the unseen world, the servant could use the same excuse. Nasafi clarifies that this excuse applies only when one is prevented from power altogether, not when one neglects to use power given by preferring something else.

Key Terms
طاعة
قوة الطاعة
ولاية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if the Mu'tazilite answers 'no' to Nasafi's counter-objection?
A) The saint has no power at all.
B) A great loneliness occurs.
C) The question becomes impossible.
D) They admit the Maturidi position.

Key Takeaway
The counter-objection forces opponents to either accept an unsavory implication or admit that power is simultaneous with the act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an act of obedience—prayer, charity, fasting—do you truly believe that the ability to do it comes from God at that very moment, or do you fall into the illusion that it is your own independent effort? How would your heart change if you constantly reminded yourself that your obedience is sustained only by His power?
?
The opponent faces a 'great loneliness' if they deny God's empowerment in the act. Have you ever felt that loneliness—a spiritual dryness when you tried to obey but sensed no divine help? What does that experience teach you about your utter dependence on Allah?
?
This theological debate is not merely academic. If you affirm that God gives power exactly in the act, how does that reshape your daily practical reliance on Him? Can you name one specific act of obedience today where you will consciously acknowledge that the power for it comes from Him at that moment?

Excuses: When 'I Could Not' Is Valid
Nasafi clarifies the conditions for a valid excuse of incapacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand when the excuse 'I was not able' is acceptable and when it is not, according to Nasafi's distinction between prevention and neglect.

Core Idea
'I could not' excuses only when power is absent, not when it is misused.

The opponents argue that in the witnessed world, the best excuse is 'I could not,' so in the unseen world the servant might use the same excuse. Nasafi clarifies that this excuse only applies when one is prevented from having power, not when one wastes the power given. Let's examine this important distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about excuses
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the contrast between 'witnessed' (شاهد) and 'unseen' (غائب).
The opponent is drawing an analogy between worldly and religious excuses.
2
Understand the opponent's argument
The opponent claims that the greatest excuse in this world is 'I was not able,' so similarly in the unseen, the servant could say that.
They want to argue that humans have a valid excuse for sin if power is simultaneous.
3
Learn Nasafi's distinction
Nasafi responds that the excuse works only when one is prevented from power altogether (منع عنه القدرة), not when one wastes power by choosing something else (ضيعها بإيثاره بدله).
The key is whether the power was given but misused.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قولهم هل اتقى أحد معصية الله تعالى وهو قادر عليها؟ فنقول: إن عنيت بالقدرة قدرة الآلات والأسباب فنعم، وإن عنيت حقيقة قدرة الفعل كان السؤال محالاً، لأن تلك القدرة توجد مع الفعل فكأنك قلت: هل اتقى أحد معصية الله تعالى وهو فاعل لها وهذا محال. ثم يقابل بمثل هذا التشنيع فيقال: هل أعطى الله تعالى وليا قوة الطاعة بعين الطاعة؟ فإن قال: لا، تمكنت وحشة عظيمة، وإن قال: نعم، أقر بقولنا، فإن قالوا: لا عذر للعبد في الشاهد أعظم من أن يقول: لو قيل له لم لا فعلت كذا؟ فيقول: لأنني لم أقدر عليه، فمثله في الغائب. قلنا: هذا يكون عذرًا فيما منع عنه القدرة لا فيما ضيعها بإيثاره بدله.
As for their saying: 'Did anyone avoid a sin of God while being capable of it?' We say: If you mean by power the power of instruments and causes, then yes; if you mean the true power of the act, then the question is impossible, because that power exists with the act, as if you said: 'Did anyone avoid a sin of God while being a doer of it?' And this is impossible. Then it is countered with a similar objection: 'Did God give a saint the power of obedience exactly in the act of obedience?' If he says no, a great loneliness occurs; if he says yes, he admits our position. And if they say: There is no greater excuse for a servant in the witnessed world than to say, when asked 'Why did you not do such-and-such?', 'Because I was not able to do it', and the like in the unseen. We say: This is an excuse concerning that from which he is prevented from power, not concerning that which he wasted by preferring something else to it.

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer (istishkāl wa jawāb) followed by a counter-objection. Nasafi first addresses the Mu'tazilite objection: 'Did anyone ever avoid a sin while being capable of it?' The objection implies that if power is simultaneous with the act, then at the moment of sinning one is capable of it, so avoiding it would require power while not sinning, leading to contradiction. Goodness of Obedience vs. Bodily Goodness
Argues that goodness of obedience is rational and superior, refuting Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between sensory goodness (of bodies) and rational goodness (of obedience), and the argument that the Mu'tazila are inconsistent in denying bodily evil while affirming bodily goodness.

Core Idea
The goodness of obedience is rational and unchanging; bodily goodness is sensory and variable.

This passage presents a key argument in the debate about the nature of goodness. The author asserts that acts of obedience have a higher, rational goodness, while bodily goodness is merely sensory and therefore variable. He then criticizes the Mu'tazila for an inconsistency: they deny that bodies can be evil, yet they claim some bodies are good. This, he argues, forces them to deny sense perception altogether.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author uses a logical structure: assertion, reason, criticism, implication, contrast.
2
Identify the two kinds of goodness
The passage distinguishes between the goodness of bodies (حسن الأجسام) and the goodness of acts of obedience (حسن الطاعات). Note how each is defined.
Bodily goodness is sensory and varies; obedience's goodness is rational and stable.
3
Analyze the critique of Mu'tazila
The author argues that the Mu'tazila, by denying the evil of bodies, undermine their own claim about the goodness of bodies. See the logical consequence: they must deny sense perception.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum argument.
4
Reflect on the rational goodness
Understand why the author considers rational goodness superior: it does not vary with intellects or change over time.
Contrast this with the variable sensory goodness.
5
Consider the incomplete ending
The passage cuts off at 'وتفاضل'. Think about what the author might have intended to conclude about superiority.
The argument is incomplete, but the direction is clear: the goodness of obedience is superior precisely because it is rational.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : لا شك أن حسن الطاعات فوق حسن الأجسام، لأن حسنها حسن وهو يختلف باختلاف الحواس على أن عند المعتزلة لا وجه للقول بحسن الأجسام لإنكارهم قبح ما قبح منها وإن عرف ذلك بالحس، فكذا يلزمهم إنكار الحس إذ لا يعرف إلا بما يعرف به القبح، ولا حقيقة لتلك المعرفة فكذا لهذه، وحسن الطاعات عقلي وهو مما لا يختلف باختلاف العقول ولا يستبدل بحال وحال، وتفاضل
Then we say: There is no doubt that the goodness of acts of obedience is above the goodness of bodies, because their goodness is goodness and it varies with the difference of senses. However, according to the Mu'tazila there is no basis for speaking of the goodness of bodies because they deny the evil of what is evil among them, even though that is known by sense. So likewise it is necessary for them to deny sense, since it is only known by that by which evil is known, and there is no reality to that knowledge, so likewise to this. And the goodness of acts of obedience is rational and it is not such that it varies with the difference of intellects nor does it change from state to state, and superiority...
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage is an argument about the nature of goodness. The author asserts that the goodness of acts of obedience (الطاعات) is superior to that of bodies. He gives a reason: the goodness of bodies is sensory and varies with different senses. However, the Mu'tazila have no basis to claim that bodies are good because they also deny that some bodies are evil (i.e., they deny the concept of evil in bodies), even though such judgments come from sense perception. This, the author argues, forces the Mu'tazila to deny the reliability of sense perception altogether; because if the knowledge of bodily evil (which is known by sense) is invalid, then the knowledge of bodily goodness (also known by sense) would be equally invalid. In contrast, the goodness of acts of obedience is rational (عقلي) and does not vary with different intellects or change over time. The argument cuts off at 'وتفاضل' (and superiority), leaving the conclusion incomplete.

Key Terms
حسن الطاعات
حسن الأجسام
الحسن العقلي
الحس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Goodness is only known through the senses.
Correction: The author argues that the goodness of obedience is known rationally and does not vary, while bodily goodness is sensory and variable.

Checkpoint
What consequence does the author draw from the Mu'tazila's denial of bodily evil (قبح الأجسام)?
A) They must accept bodily goodness
B) They must deny sense perception entirely
C) They must affirm rational goodness
D) They must reject the Qur'an

Key Takeaway
The goodness of obedience is rational and unchanging, while bodily goodness is sensory and variable; the Mu'tazila are inconsistent in affirming bodily goodness while denying bodily evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The Mu'tazila were inconsistent: they accepted bodily goodness but denied bodily evil. Where in your own life do you accept a standard for others but reject it for yourself? How does this challenge you to be consistent in your moral reasoning?
?
The author says the goodness of obedience is rational and unchanging. Does your obedience to Allah fluctuate with your moods and situations? What one change would make your worship less dependent on your senses and more on your intellect?

Why Knowledge Does Not Necessitate Faith
Refuting the claim that necessary knowledge necessitates belief

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the existence of necessary knowledge or proofs does not automatically produce faith in a person, and how the author uses Quranic verses to support this.

Core Idea
Knowing the truth is not the same as believing it.

This lesson tackles a common theological claim: that if God gives someone undeniable proof and necessary knowledge of Islam, that person will inevitably believe. The author of this passage refutes this by showing that knowledge and faith are two distinct realities, and that the Quran itself testifies that disbelievers can have clear knowledge yet choose not to believe. You will see how he weaves together several Quranic verses to ground his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then read the full translation. Notice the author's main claim and the Quranic evidence he cites.
The passage starts by stating the opposing view, then declares it false.
2
Identify the key distinction
The author argues that knowledge of faith is distinct from faith itself. Find where he says this and understand why this distinction matters.
3
Examine the Quranic evidence
The author cites several verses (6:111, 6:25, 52:44, 2:146). For each verse, see how it supports the claim that proofs do not force belief.
Pay attention to how the author extracts two aspects from 6:111.
4
Trace the conclusion
The passage ends with 'So it is established that the existence of faith and guidance is not a necessary consequence of knowledge and the proof.' Confirm that the evidence leads to this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن يخلق الله تعالى في العبد العلم الضروري بصحة الإسلام وحقيقته ويقيم له الدلائل المثبتة له العلم الضروري بذلك فيؤمنوا حينئذ. وهذا فاسد لأن العلم بصحة الإيمان وحقيقة الدين غير الدين والإيمان، وليس من ضرورة وجود أحد المتغايرين وجود الآخر لا محالة، بل من الجائز أنه لا يوجد ألا ترى أن الله تعالى قال : وَلَوْ أَنَّنَا نَزَّلْنَا إِلَيْهِمُ الْمَلَائِكَةَ وَكَلَّمَهُمُ الْمَوْتَى وَحَشَرْنَا عَلَيْهِمْ كُلَّ شَيْءٍ قُبُلًا مَّا كَانُوا لِيُؤْمِنُوا إِلَّا أَن يَشَاءَ اللَّهُ أخبر أنه وإن أقام كل دليل لا يؤمنون إلا أن يشاء الله تعالى إيمانهم فكان في الآية وجهان ينبئان عن بطلان هذا الكلام. أحدهما أن قيام هذه الدلائل غير ومشيئة الإيمان غير حيث قال عز وجل: مَا كَانُوا لِيُؤْمِنُوا إِلَّا أَن يَشَاءَ الله بعد وجود هذه الدلائل. والآخر: أنه تعالى أثبت أن بقيام هذه الدلائل لا يؤمنون فثبت أن ليس من ضرورة ثبوت الدليل أو العلم به ثبوت الإيمان، وكذا قال : وَإِن يَرَوْا كُلَّ آيَةٍ لَا يُؤْمِنُوا بها فمن قال يؤمنون بها محالة فقد كذب الله تعالى في خبره وقال تعالى: ﴿ وَإِن يَرَوْا كِسْفًا مِنَ السَّمَاءِ سَاقِطًا يَقُولُوا سَحَابٌ مَرْكُومٌ يحققه : أن أهل العناد لم يكونوا يؤمنون وإن كان العلم بطريق الحقيقة ثابتا لهم كما قال تعالى: يَعْرِفُونَهُ كَمَا يَعْرِفُونَ أَبْنَاءَهُمْ وَإِنَّ فَرِيقًا مِّنْهُمْ لَيَكْتُمُونَ الْحَقَّ وَهُمْ يَعْلَمُونَ . فثبت أن وجود الإيمان والهداية ليس من ضرورة العلم والدليل
That God Most High creates in the servant necessary knowledge of the soundness of Islam and its reality, and establishes for him proofs that affirm for him necessary knowledge of that, so that they then believe. This is false, because knowledge of the soundness of faith and the reality of religion is other than religion and faith, and it is not necessary from the existence of one of two distinct things the existence of the other necessarily; rather it is possible that it does not exist. Do you not see that God Most High said: 'And even if We sent down to them the angels and the dead spoke to them and We gathered against them every thing face to face, they would not believe unless God wills.' He informed that even if He establishes every proof, they would not believe unless God Most High wills their faith. So in the verse there are two aspects that indicate the falsity of this statement. One: that the establishment of these proofs is other than God's willing of faith, where He said: 'They would not believe unless God wills' after the existence of these proofs. The other: that He Most High affirmed that with the establishment of these proofs they do not believe, so it is established that it is not necessary from the existence of the proof or knowledge of it the existence of faith. Similarly He said: 'And if they see every sign, they will not believe in it.' So whoever says they will believe in it necessarily has belied God Most High in His report. And He Most High said: 'And if they see a fragment from the sky falling, they say: Clouds piled up.' This is verified by the fact that the obstinate people would not believe even though knowledge by way of reality was established for them, as God Most High said: 'They recognize it as they recognize their own sons, and indeed a party of them conceal the truth while they know.' So it is established that the existence of faith and guidance is not a necessary consequence of knowledge and the proof.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a claim made by some (likely the Mu'tazila or earlier figures) that God creates necessary knowledge of the truth of Islam in a person and provides proofs, so that the person then necessarily believes. The author declares this false by distinguishing between knowledge and faith themselves: they are two distinct things; one does not necessitate the other. He then cites Quran 6:111, which states that even if angels were sent down, the dead spoke, and everything was gathered, they would not believe unless God wills. This verse shows two key points: (1) the existence of proofs is separate from God's willing of faith, and (2) the existence of proofs does not force faith to occur. He further cites Quran 6:25 and 52:44 to show that even when signs are seen, disbelievers still do not believe and even mock. Finally, he invokes Quran 2:146, where the People of the Book recognize the truth but conceal it, confirming that knowledge alone does not lead to faith. Thus the conclusion is established: faith and guidance are not necessary consequences of knowledge or proof.

Key Terms
علم ضروري
إيمان
مشيئة
دليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a person is given undeniable proof and necessary knowledge of Islam, they will inevitably believe.
Correction: Knowledge and faith are distinct; the presence of proof does not force belief, as Quranic verses show that even with clear signs and recognition, some choose not to believe.

Checkpoint
What is the main basis the author uses to refute the claim that necessary knowledge leads to faith?
A) Rational deduction
B) Quranic verses
C) Consensus of scholars
D) Personal experience

Key Takeaway
Faith is not a necessary consequence of knowledge; the Quran repeatedly shows that people can know the truth and still reject it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that Islam is true. You have seen proofs. Yet are there areas in your life where you are not submitting to that truth? What specific area are you holding back, and what would it cost you to surrender it fully to Allah today?
?
The passage says that even the People of the Book recognized the truth as they recognize their own sons, yet they concealed it. Is there any truth about your own character or sins that you recognize but conceal from others or even from yourself? What would it mean for you to stop concealing and repent now?
?
If faith does not automatically follow from knowledge, then what is keeping you from having stronger faith? Are you depending on more proofs, or are you lacking the humility to ask Allah for the will to believe? What will you do tonight to sincerely ask for that gift?

The Hadith on Qadr and the Mu'tazila as Opponents of God
Nasafi cites hadith to prove Mu'tazila are opponents of God and worse than Magians.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi uses the hadith on Qadr to identify the Mu'tazila as opponents of God who deny His full sovereignty.

Core Idea
Denying that both good and evil are from God makes one an opponent of God.

In this passage, Nasafi presents a powerful argument against the Mu'tazila. He cites a hadith that affirms both good and evil are from God, and he shows that the Mu'tazila, by denying this, align themselves with the Magians and become 'opponents of God.' This lesson unpacks the logic of that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith citation
Read the opening where Nasafi cites the hadith 'Qadr, its good and its evil, is from God.' Note that this hadith is presented as a condition of faith.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila error
Nasafi claims the Mu'tazila assisted the Magians in denying this hadith. Recognize that this places them outside the bounds of Islamic orthodoxy.
3
Examine the counter-argument
Nasafi argues that the very hadiths the Mu'tazila narrate prove that they are the true Qadariyya, not their opponents. Notice how he contrasts their position with that of Ahl al-Sunna.
4
Understand the key difference
Ahl al-Sunna affirm God's creation of acts but do not dispute His justice; the Mu'tazila claim human creation and then argue with God. This makes them opponents of God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيد هذا ما رواه غير واحد عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم "القدر خيره وشره من الله تعالى". وروى أنه جعل هذا من شرط الإيمان والمجوس يكذبونه في ذلك وساعدت المعتزلة المجوس على تكذيب النبي عليه السلام في ذلك، وما روت المعتزلة من الحديث من أدل الدلائل على أن الاسم واقع عليهم دون خصمائهم فإن خصماءهم وإن قالوا إن الله تعالى هو الخالق لأفعالهم إلا أنهم لا يخاصمونه في التعذيب على ما قبح منها بل يرون ذلك حكمة وعدلاً، وكذا تخليقه على ما مر قبل هذا، وكذا لا يطالبونه بثواب ما أتوا به من الطاعات ويقولون ما أدينا إلا حقك
And this is supported by what more than one narrated from the Prophet, peace be upon him: 'Qadr, its good and its evil, is from God most high.' And it is narrated that He made this a condition of faith, and the Magians deny that, and the Mu'tazila assisted the Magians in denying the Prophet, peace be upon him, in that. And what the Mu'tazila narrated of the hadith is among the strongest proofs that the name (Qadariyya) applies to them and not to their opponents, for their opponents, even though they say that God most high is the Creator of their acts, yet they do not dispute with Him regarding punishment for what is ugly among them; rather they consider that wisdom and justice, and likewise His creation as passed before this, and likewise they do not demand from Him reward for the acts of obedience they perform, saying: We have only rendered Your due.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage provides further evidence that the Mu'tazila are the true opponents of God. Nasafi cites the well-known hadith 'Qadr, its good and its evil, is from God most high,' which is considered a condition of faith. The Magians deny this hadith, and the Mu'tazila ally with them in this denial. Nasafi then argues that the very hadiths that the Mu'tazila themselves narrate (distorting them) actually prove that the name 'Qadariyya' applies to them, not to their opponents. The opponents (Ahl al-Sunna) affirm that God creates all human acts, but they do not dispute with God regarding punishment for evil acts—they see it as just and wise—nor do they demand reward for obedience, recognizing that all is God's due. The Mu'tazila, by contrast, claim that humans create their own acts, then complain about God's justice, making them opponents of God. The passage reinforces that the Mu'tazila are worse than the Magians because they not only deny God's power over evil but also argue with God about His justice.

Key Terms
القدر
الحديث
المعتزلة خصماء الله
التكذيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ahl al-Sunna dispute with God about justice because they assert human responsibility.
Correction: Ahl al-Sunna affirm that God creates all acts and they do not question His justice; they accept punishment as wise and do not demand reward.

Checkpoint
According to the hadith cited by Nasafi, what is the correct belief about qadr?
A) The good is from God but the evil is from Satan.
B) Both good and evil are from God.
C) Humans create their own actions independently.
D) God only creates good, not evil.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila are opponents of God because they deny that both good and evil are from God and dispute His justice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When evil befalls you, do you ever find yourself silently disputing with God, questioning His justice? How does this hadith—that both good and evil are from Him—challenge you to surrender your complaints?
?
The Mu'tazila assisted the Magians in denying the Prophet. Are there people or ideologies in your life whose rejection of Islamic teachings you are unwittingly supporting, perhaps through silence or companionship? What would it cost you to distance yourself from them?
?
The Ahl al-Sunna say: 'We have only rendered Your due'—they do not demand reward from God. Is your worship driven by a sense of entitlement to Paradise, or by genuine servitude? What would change in your prayer and charity if you truly believed you owe God everything?
