Proof that the Grave Sinner Will Definitely Enter Paradise
Nasafi argues from Quranic promises that believers with sins will enter Paradise eventually.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi uses Quranic promises to prove that even believers who commit major sins will eventually enter Paradise.

Core Idea
The Quran promises Paradise to believers with good deeds, and a grave sinner is still a believer with good deeds.

Nasafi has already established that faith does not leave a person due to major sins. Now he draws the natural conclusion: such a believer will definitely enter Paradise. He supports this with numerous clear Quranic verses that promise Paradise to believers who do righteous deeds — and the grave sinner, despite his sins, has both faith and righteous deeds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and grasp the argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Nasafi first restates his premise (faith remains with major sins) and then derives the conclusion that the grave sinner will enter Paradise. The evidence is a series of Quranic verses.
The passage is concise; each verse is cited to show the promise applies to believers who do good.
2
Identify how the Quranic verses support the conclusion
List each verse Nasafi cites. For each, note the key phrase that connects to the grave sinner: all promise Paradise to believers who perform righteous deeds. The grave sinner qualifies because he has faith and some good deeds.
The verses collectively show that the promise is general and not negated by sins.
3
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider why this argument refutes the Khawarij and Mu'tazila positions. They claimed grave sinners are eternally in Hell. Nasafi shows that the Quranic promises overrule that, because the sinner is still a believer and has good deeds.
This establishes the Maturidi position that grave sinners will eventually enter Paradise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إذا ثبت أن الإيمان لا يزول بما دون التكذيب وأنه مع ارتكاب الكبيرة باق فنقول : إنه يدخل الجنة لا محالة لما ورد من الوعد للمؤمنين بالثواب في دار الآخرة في كثير من الآيات من نحو قوله تعالى: ﴿ إِنَّ الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا وَعَمِلُوا الصَّالِحَاتِ كَانَتْ لَهُمْ جَنَّاتُ الْفِرْدَوْسِ نُزُلاً ﴾ وصاحب الكبيرة مؤمن وقد عمل صالحات كثيرة ﴿ إِنَّ الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا وَعَمِلُوا الصَّالِحَاتِ هُمْ خَيْرُ الْبَرِيَّةِ ﴾ وقوله : ﴿ إِلَّا مَنْ آمَنَ وَعَمِلَ صَالِحًا فَأُولَئِكَ لَهُمْ جَزَاءُ النِّعْمَةِ بِمَا عَمِلُوا وَهُمْ فِي الْغُرُفَاتِ آمِنُونَ ﴾ وقوله: ﴿ فَمَنْ يَعْمَلْ مِثْقَالَ ذَرَّةٍ خَيْرًا يَرَهُ ﴾ وقوله : ﴿ وَمَنْ يَعْمَلْ مِنَ الصَّالِحَاتِ مِنْ ذَكَرٍ أَوْ أُنْثَى وَهُوَ مُؤْمِنٌ ﴾ وقوله : ﴿ مَنْ عَمِلَ سَيِّئَةً فَلَا يُجْزَى إِلَّا مِثْلَهَا وَمَنْ عَمِلَ صَالِحًا مِنْ ذَكَرٍ أَوْ أُنْثَى وَهُوَ مُؤْمِنٌ فَأُولَئِكَ يَدْخُلُونَ الْجَنَّةَ يُرْزَقُونَ فِيهَا بِغَيْرِ حِسَابٍ ﴾ وقوله : ﴿ مَنْ جَاءَ بِالْحَسَنَةِ فَلَهُ عَشْرُ أَمْثَالِهَا وَمَنْ جَاءَ بِالسَّيِّئَةِ فَلَا يُجْزَى إِلَّا مِثْلَهَا ﴾ الآية. في آيات لا تحصى.
Then, when it is established that faith does not disappear with what is less than denial, and that it remains with the commission of a major sin, we say: He will definitely enter Paradise because of the promise that has come to the believers of reward in the Abode of the Hereafter in many verses, such as His saying: 'Indeed those who have believed and done righteous deeds, for them are the Gardens of Paradise as a lodging.' And the possessor of a major sin is a believer and has done many righteous deeds. [And His saying:] 'Indeed those who have believed and done righteous deeds, they are the best of creatures.' And His saying: 'Except those who believe and do righteous deeds, then for them is the reward of bliss for what they did, and they in the lofty chambers are secure.' And His saying: 'So whoever does an atom's weight of good will see it.' And His saying: 'And whoever does righteous deeds, male or female, while he is a believer...' And His saying: 'Whoever does an evil deed will not be recompensed except the like of it, and whoever does righteousness, male or female, while he is a believer, those will enter Paradise, provided for therein without account.' And His saying: 'Whoever comes with a good deed will have ten times its like, and whoever comes with an evil deed will not be recompensed except the like of it' the verse. In countless verses.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage provides the second key conclusion in the discussion of grave sins: the person who commits a major sin (mur'takib al-kabira) will definitely enter Paradise in the end. Nasafi builds on the previous section where he established that faith does not disappear due to anything less than outright denial (takdhib) and that faith remains even when major sins are committed. Now he argues: since this person is a believer and has performed many righteous deeds, the numerous Quranic promises of Paradise for believers who do good works apply directly to him. He cites several verses: 'Indeed those who have believed and done righteous deeds, for them are the Gardens of Paradise as a lodging' (al-Kahf 107); 'Indeed those who have believed and done righteous deeds, they are the best of creatures' (al-Bayyinah 7); the exception clause in Surah Saba' 37 that those who believe and do good will have the reward of bliss and be secure in lofty chambers; the verse from al-Zalzalah 7 that even an atom's weight of good will be seen; the verse in al-Nisa' 124 that whoever does righteous deeds while being a believer will enter Paradise; the verse in Ghafir 40 that whoever does a good deed while a believer will enter Paradise with provision without account; and the verse in al-An'am 160 that a good deed is multiplied ten times while an evil deed is only recompensed equally. Nasafi emphasizes that such verses are countless. The point is clear: the grave sinner has faith and at least some good deeds, so he falls under the general promise to believers. Even if he is punished in Hell for his sins, he will eventually be brought out and enter Paradise. This is the position of Ahl al-Sunnah against the Khawarij and Mu'tazila who held that grave sinners would be eternally in Hell.

Correction: The Quranic promises show that because the sinner is still a believer with good deeds, he will eventually enter Paradise.

Checkpoint
What is the main basis for the claim that the grave sinner will definitely enter Paradise?
A) His faith alone, even without good deeds.
B) The general promise of Paradise for believers who do good deeds, and he has both faith and good deeds.
C) The intercession of the Prophet.
D) The fact that he repented before death.

Key Takeaway
The grave sinner, being a believer with good deeds, falls under the Quranic promise of Paradise, ensuring his eventual entry.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that your sins, no matter how many, will not keep you out of Paradise if you are a believer who does good deeds? How does this certainty affect your relationship with Allah today?
?
If every good deed is multiplied and every sin is only recompensed equally, why do you still hesitate to do good and fear sin? What one sin are you holding onto that you know you must leave?
?
The Quranic promise is conditional on faith and righteous deeds. Right now, are you actively fulfilling that condition? What is the smallest righteous deed you can commit before the day ends to claim that promise?

The Believer Committing Major Sin Enters Paradise
Nasafi proves the grave sinner enters Paradise with Quranic promises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi uses Quranic promises to argue that a believer who commits a major sin will ultimately enter Paradise.

Core Idea
Faith with major sins still wins Paradise because Allah's promise is unconditional for believers.

In the previous lesson, it was established that faith does not vanish with sins below disbelief. Now, Nasafi draws the positive conclusion: the believer who commits a major sin will inevitably enter Paradise. The argument is simple but powerful — it relies on the numerous Quranic verses that promise Paradise to those who believe and do righteous deeds. Let's examine these proofs.

Guided Path
1
Recall the premise
Before reading this passage, remember that Nasafi has already proven that faith remains with major sins. This is the foundation for his conclusion.
2
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how Nasafi states the conclusion first, then provides scriptural evidence.
3
Identify the key verses
List the Quranic verses cited. Each one emphasizes that belief combined with righteous deeds leads to Paradise.
4
Understand the logic
The implicit logic: since the person is a believer and has done righteous deeds (including faith), the promise applies despite the major sin. The sin does not negate the promise.
5
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this argument counters those who say major sins nullify faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إذا ثبت أن الإيمان لا يزول بما دون التكذيب وأنه مع ارتكاب الكبيرة باق فنقول : إنه يدخل الجنة لا محالة لما ورد من الوعد للمؤمنين بالثواب في دار الآخرة في كثير من الآيات من نحو قوله تعالى: ﴿ إِنَّ الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا وَعَمِلُوا الصَّلِحَتِ كَانَتْ لَهُمْ جَنَّتُ الْفِرْدَوْسِ نُزُلاً ﴾ وقوله: ﴿ إِنَّ الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا وَعَمِلُوا الصَّلِحَتِ هُمْ جَنَّتْ تَجْرِى مِن تحتها الأنهر ذلك الفوز الكبير ﴾ وقوله: ﴿ إِنَّ الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا وَعَمِلُوا الصَّلِحَتِ أُولَيكَ هُمْ خَيْرُ البَريَّة ﴾ وقوله: ﴿ إِلَّا مَنْ ءَامَنَ وَعَمِلَ صَلِحًا فَأُولَيكَ لَهُم جَزَاءُ النِّعَفِ بِمَا عَمِلُوا وَهُمْ فِي الْغُرُفَاتِ عَامِنُونَ ﴾ وقوله: ﴿ فَمَن يَعْمَلْ مِثْقَالَ ذَرَّةٍ خَيْرًا يَرَهُ ﴾ وقوله: ﴿ وَمَن يَعْمَلْ مِنَ الصَّالِحَتِ مِن ذَكَرٍ أَوْ أُنثَى وَهُوَ مُؤْمِنٌ ﴾ وقوله: ﴿ مَنْ عَمِلَ سَيِّئَةٌ فَلَا يُجْزَى إِلَّا مِثْلَهَا وَمَنْ عَمِلَ صَلِحاً مِّن ذَكَرٍ أَوْ أُنقَ وَهُوَ مُؤْمِنٌ فَأُولَيكَ يدخلون الجنة يرزَقُونَ فِيهَا بِغَيْرِ حِسَابٍ ﴾ وقوله: ﴿ مَن جَاءَ بِالحَسَنَةِ فَلَهُ عَشْرُ أَمْثَالِهَا وَمَن جَاءَ بِالسَّيِّئَةِ فَلَا يُجْزَى إِلَّا مِثْلَهَا ﴾ في آيات لا تحصى.
Then if it is established that faith does not disappear by what is below denial, and that with the commission of a major sin it remains, we say: He enters Paradise inevitably because of the promise that has come to the believers of reward in the abode of the Hereafter in many verses, such as His saying, the Exalted: 'Indeed, those who have believed and done righteous deeds, for them will be the Garden of Paradise as a lodging' and His saying: 'Indeed, those who have believed and done righteous deeds, for them are Gardens beneath which rivers flow; that is the great attainment' and His saying: 'Indeed, those who have believed and done righteous deeds, they are the best of creatures' and His saying: 'Except those who believe and do righteous deeds, for them will be the reward of kindness for what they did, and they in the lofty chambers will be secure' and His saying: 'So whoever does an atom's weight of good will see it' and His saying: 'And whoever does righteous deeds, male or female, while being a believer' and His saying: 'Whoever does an evil deed will not be requited except the like thereof, and whoever does righteous deeds, male or female, while being a believer, those will enter Paradise, provided therein without account' and His saying: 'Whoever comes with a good deed will have ten times the like thereof, and whoever comes with an evil deed will not be requited except the like thereof' in countless verses.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage is a proof from revelation (Quranic promises) that a believer who commits a major sin will ultimately enter Paradise. Nasafi first restates the premise established earlier—that faith does not vanish with sins below disbelief, and that it remains with the commission of a major sin. He then draws the conclusion: such a person will inevitably enter Paradise. The proof is grounded in the many Quranic verses that unconditionally promise Paradise to those who believe and do righteous deeds. Since the person in question is a believer and has performed many righteous acts (including the supreme act of faith), these promises apply. Nasafi cites eight verses, all emphasizing that belief combined with good deeds results in Paradise. The verses cover various aspects: the Gardens of Paradise, the best of creatures, security in lofty chambers, reward for good deeds, and the multiplication of good deeds. The implicit argument is that the major sin does not negate the effect of these promises, because faith and good deeds are present. The phrase 'in countless verses' underscores the overwhelming scriptural support.

Rational Argument for the Grave Sinner's Salvation
Nasafi gives a rational argument comparing good and evil deeds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's rational argument that a believer who commits a major sin cannot be eternally punished because it would violate God's promise to multiply good deeds.

Core Idea
Eternal punishment for a grave sinner would break God's promise to multiply good deeds.

Nasafi presents a powerful rational argument, not merely scriptural, to show that a believer with faith cannot be eternally punished for limited sins. He uses the logic of God's promises: God promised to multiply good deeds many times over, but only punish evil deeds proportionally. Eternal punishment would nullify that promise, which is logically and morally untenable.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify its structure
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once. Notice that Nasafi starts by describing the scenario: a believer has the best good deed (tasdiq) and many righteous works, but only limited sins.
2
Understand the core argument
Nasafi argues that if this believer is eternally punished, it would mean increasing the punishment for evil (beyond its like) and decreasing the reward for good (not multiplying it), which constitutes a breach of promise (khalaf fi al-wa'd).
The argument is not about weighing deeds but about God's promises.
3
Examine the scriptural evidence
Nasafi cites Quranic verses (2:261, 2:245, etc.) that promise multiplication of good deeds. He uses these to show that eternal punishment would contradict the clear promise of multiplied reward.
The opponents (Mu'tazilites) accept that God cannot break His threat, so Nasafi turns their own principle against them.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The only consistent conclusion is that the grave sinner will eventually be saved, preserving God's promise and justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن هذا الرجل أتى بما هو أفضل الخيرات وهو التصديق، والشر الذي أتى به لا يبلغ نهاية الجحود والمعاندة، فإذا أتى هو بما هو نهاية في الخيرات وبكثير من الأعمال الصالحة بل بما لا يحصى كثرة، ولم يأت بما هو نهاية من الشرور، فإذا خلد في النار وأبطل ثواب أفضل الخيرات ونهايتها، وما أتى به من الصالحات بارتكاب ما ليس نهاية في الشرور ولا له كثرة بل ارتكب ذلك مرة أو مرارا محصورة مع ما اقترن به مما هو عبادة عظيمة من خوف العقوبة ورجاء عفو خالقه عنه بسعة رحمته فقد زيد في عقاب الشرور بل عقاب شر واحد، ونقص من ثواب الخيرات وفيه خلف في الوعد، فإنه تعالى وعد أن يجزي الحسنة بعشرة أمثالها والسيئة بمثل بل وعد سبعمائة بقوله تعالى: مَّثَلُ الَّذِينَ يُنفِقُونَ أَمْوَالَهُمْ فِي سَبِيلِ اللَّهِ كَمَثَلِ حَبَّةٍ أَنْبَتَتْ سَبْعَ سَنَابِلَ فِي كُلِّ سُنْبُلَةٍ مِائَةُ حَبَّةٍ ثم لم يكتف بذلك بل وعد أضعافا مضاعفة بقوله: وَاللَّهُ يُضَعِفُ لِمَن يَشَاءُ وقوله: مَن ذَا الَّذِي يُقْرِضُ اللَّهَ قَرْضًا حسنا فيضعِفَهُ لَهُم أَضْعَافًا كَثِيرَةً وهم لا يجوزون الخلف في الوعيد فكيف يجوزون الخلف في الوعد، وهو بإجماع العقلاء من أمارات اللوم وهو قبيح في نفسه، فإذا على زعمهم ما اقتصر في السيئات على جزاء مثلها بل زاد عليها ما لا نهاية، ولم يجز على حسنة مثلها فضلاً عن العشرة وسبعمائة، وهذا هو الخلف الذي ليس وراءه خلف.
Then indeed this man has brought what is the best of good deeds — namely, affirmation (tasdiq) — and the evil that he has brought does not reach the ultimate of denial and obstinacy. So when he has brought what is ultimate in good deeds and many righteous works, indeed countless, and has not brought what is ultimate in evils, then if he is eternally punished in Fire and the reward of the best of good deeds and their ultimate is nullified, along with the righteous deeds he has brought, due to his commission of what is not ultimate in evils nor has multiplicity, but rather he committed it once or a limited number of times, accompanied by what is a great act of worship — fear of punishment and hope for forgiveness from his Creator by the vastness of His mercy — then the punishment of evils, indeed the punishment of a single evil, has been increased, and the reward of good deeds has been decreased, and therein is a breach of promise (khalaf fi al-wa'd). For He, exalted, promised to recompense the good deed with ten times its like and the evil deed with its like, indeed He promised seven hundredfold by His saying: 'The example of those who spend their wealth in the way of Allah is like a seed that grows seven spikes; in each spike is a hundred seeds.' Then He was not content with that, but promised multiplied multiples by His saying: 'And Allah multiplies for whom He wills' and His saying: 'Who is it that would loan Allah a goodly loan so that He may multiply it for them many times?' And they do not allow breach of promise (khalaf) in the threat (wa'id), so how can they allow breach of promise in the promise (wa'd)? And that, by consensus of the rational people, is among the signs of blameworthiness and is ugly in itself. So then, according to their claim, He did not limit the evil deeds to recompense the like thereof, but rather increased upon them without limit, and He did not recompense a good deed even with its like, let alone tenfold and seven hundredfold; and this is the breach of promise beyond which there is no breach.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage presents a rational argument (not merely scriptural) for why a believer who commits a major sin cannot be eternally punished. Nasafi constructs an analogy: the person has performed the best of good deeds (tasdiq or affirmation of faith), which is the ultimate good, while his sin does not reach the ultimate evil (which would be disbelief or obstinate denial). He has many good deeds, but only limited sins. If God were to eternally punish him, that would mean nullifying the reward of the ultimate good and many good deeds, while only punishing a limited sin (which does not reach the ultimate and is not multiplied). This would involve increasing the punishment for evil and decreasing the reward for good, which constitutes a breach of God's promise (khalaf fi al-wa'd) because God has promised to multiply good deeds (e.g., ten times, seven hundredfold, or more) and to only punish an evil deed with its like. The argument is polemical: the opponents (Mu'tazilites) deny that God can break His threat (wa'id) regarding punishment of grave sinners, but they are here accused of allowing a breach of promise (wa'd) by not fulfilling the promised multiplication of good deeds. Nasafi emphasizes that breach of promise is universally considered blameworthy and ugly. Therefore, the only consistent position is that the grave sinner will be forgiven or eventually enter Paradise, upholding God's promise of reward.

Key Terms
التصديق
الخيرات
الشرور
الوعد
الوعيد
الخلف
المضاعفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is about weighing good and evil deeds to decide punishment.
Correction: Nasafi's argument is not about weighing deeds but about the logical consequence of God's promise to multiply good deeds; eternal punishment would break that promise.

Checkpoint
What does Nasafi argue would be the consequence of eternally punishing a believer who committed a major sin?
A) It would be just because the sin is equally grave.
B) It would nullify God's promise to multiply good deeds and involve a breach of promise.
C) It would prove that faith is not present.
D) It would show that God is unjust.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi's rational argument shows that eternal punishment for a believer with major sins is impossible because it would contradict God's promise to multiply good deeds.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that God has promised to multiply your good deeds, yet you often feel that your sins outweigh them. After reading Nasafi's argument, do you still believe that God could abandon you to eternal punishment? How does this reshape your hope in His mercy and your fear of Him? What is one good deed you have neglected that you will now commit to, trusting in His multiplication?
?
Nasafi argues that eternal punishment would be a breach of God's promise, and breach is blameworthy. Have you ever doubted God's promise of forgiveness because of your repeated sins? What specific good deeds have you been neglecting because you thought they were insignificant? How will you act today on the certainty that God multiplies them?
?
If you truly internalized that God always keeps His promise to multiply good deeds, what would change in your daily worship? Would you still procrastinate in performing acts of obedience? Why do you hesitate to trust in His promise despite this rational proof? What is one concrete step you will take this week to act on this certainty?

Refuting the Accusation of Breach of Threat
Nasafi rejects the accusation that Ahl al-Haqq uphold breach of threat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nature of the accusation made against Ahl al-Haqq regarding God's threat and why Nasafi considers it shameless.

Core Idea
The accusation that Ahl al-Haqq endorse divine breach of promise is itself a shameless charge.

In this passage, Nasafi responds to a polemical accusation. The opponents claim that by permitting forgiveness for major sins, Ahl al-Haqq are effectively saying God breaks His threat. Nasafi does not accept this characterisation; he calls it one of the greatest shamelessnesses.

Guided Path
1

The passage is incomplete, but the key accusation and response are present.

The opponents equate 'forgiveness of major sins' with 'God breaking His promise.'
3
Analyze Nasafi's response
Note how Nasafi dismisses the accusation as shameless (waqaha). Consider why he might consider it an unfair distortion.
This is a rhetorical refutation, not a detailed theological argument.
4
Connect to the wider debate
Understand that this is part of a larger polemic between Ahl al-Haqq and the Mu'tazilites on the nature of God's threats and promises.
The previous lesson likely discussed khalaf in promise; here the focus is on threat.

Commentary
This short passage is a refutation of the opponents' accusation. Nasafi states that the opponents (probably the Mu'tazilites) accuse the Ahl al-Haqq (the orthodox Sunnis, i.e., Maturidis and Ash'aris) of claiming that God may break His threat (khalaf fi al-wa'id) by forgiving a grave sinner instead of punishing him. Nasafi calls this accusation 'one of the greatest shamelessnesses' (min a'zam al-waqahat). He then begins to cite verses (likely supporting the possibility of forgiveness), but the text is cut off at the end of the page. The argument is that the opponents are being hypocritical: they accuse the Ahl al-Haqq of khalaf in threat, but as shown in the previous lesson, they themselves are guilty of khalaf in promise. The passage serves as a transitional rebuttal.

Al-Nasafi's Argument Against Eternal Punishment for Believing Sinners
Al-Nasafi argues that eternal punishment for a believer would break God's promise of multiplied reward.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Nasafi's reductio ad absurdum argument against the Mu'tazila claim that believers who commit major sins are eternally punished, and how it relies on the concept of khulf (breach of promise).

Core Idea
God's promise of multiplied reward cannot be broken.

In this passage, the Maturidi theologian Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi presents a powerful argument against the Mu'tazila doctrine of eternal punishment for sinful believers. He argues that if a believer with faith and many good deeds were eternally punished, God would be breaking His own promise of multiplying rewards, which is rationally and morally impossible. The argument uses Quranic verses to demonstrate the scale of divine reward and exposes an inconsistency in the Mu'tazila position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage with translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: a case is presented, a consequence derived, Quranic evidence cited, and a conclusion drawn.
Pay attention to the footnote markers (1)-(4) - they indicate verses in the original.
2
Understand the premise
Al-Nasafi imagines a believer who performs the best deed (tasdiq/faith) and many righteous works, while his sins are few and not extreme. This sets up the scenario for the argument.
3
Identify the consequence: khulf
If such a person were eternally punished, it would mean increasing punishment for sins and decreasing reward for good deeds, which constitutes a breach of God's promise (khulf fi al-wa'd). This is the core of the argument.
Khulf is considered ugly by consensus of rational people.
4
Examine the Quranic proofs
Al-Nasafi cites verses promising multiplication of rewards: a good deed gets tenfold, spending in God's way gets 700-fold, and even more. These establish God's promise of abundant reward.
The verses are from Surah al-Baqarah 2:261 and others.
5
Reflect on the inconsistency
The Mu'tazila insist that God must fulfill His threat (wa'id) to punish sinners, but they ignore the promise of reward. Al-Nasafi points out that accepting eternal punishment for a believer would mean accepting a breach in the promise of reward, which is a greater absurdity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن هذا الرجل أتى بما هو أفضل الخيرات وهو التصديق، والشر الذي أتى به لا يبلغ نهاية الجحود والمعاندة ، فإذا أتى هو بما هو نهاية في الخيرات وبكثير من الأعمال الصالحة بل بما لا يحصى كثرة ، ولم يأت بما هو نهاية من الشرور، فإذا خلد في النار وأبطل ثواب أفضل الخيرات ونهايتها، وما أتى به من الصالحات بارتكاب ما ليس نهاية في الشرور ولا له كثرة بل ارتكب ذلك مرة أو مرارا محصورة مع ما اقترن به مما هو عبادة عظيمة من خوف العقوبة ورجاء عفو خالقه عنه بسعة رحمته فقد زيد في عقاب الشرور بل (۱) عقاب شر واحد، ونقص من ثواب الخيرات وفيه خلف في الوعد، فإنه تعالى وعد أن يجزي الحسنة بعشرة أمثالها والسيئة بمثل بل وعد سبعمائة بقوله تعالى: مَّثَلُ الَّذِينَ يُنفِقُونَ أَمْوَالَهُمْ فِي سَبِيلِ اللَّهِ كَمَثَلِ حَبَّةٍ أَنْبَتَتْ سَبْعَ سَنَابِلَ فِي كُلِّ سُنْبُلَةٍ مِائَةُ حَبَّةٍ ) (۲) ثم لم يكتف بذلك بل وعد أضعافا مضاعفة بقوله : وَاللَّهُ يُضَعِفُ لِمَن يَشَاءُ ) (۳) وقوله : مَن ذَا الَّذِي يُقْرِضُ اللَّهَ قَرْضًا حسنا فيضعِفَهُ لَهُم أَضْعَافًا كَثِيرَةً (٤) وهم لا يجوزون الخلف في الوعيد فكيف يجوزون الخلف في الوعد، وهو بإجماع العقلاء من أمارات اللوم وهو قبيح في نفسه، فإذا على زعمهم ما اقتصر في السيئات على جزاء مثلها بل زاد عليها ما لا نهاية، ولم يجز على حسنة مثلها فضلاً عن العشرة وسبعمائة، وهذا هو الخلف الذي ليس وراءه خلف.
Then indeed this man came with what is the best of good deeds, namely affirmation (al-tasdiq), and the evil he committed does not reach the extreme of obstinate denial and opposition. So when he came with what is extreme in good deeds and with many righteous works, indeed with what cannot be counted for multitude, and he did not come with what is extreme of evils, then if he is eternally in the Fire and the reward of the best good deeds and their ultimate is nullified, and the righteous deeds he performed are nullified because of committing what is not extreme in evils nor of great number, rather he committed that once or a limited number of times, accompanied by what is great worship from fear of punishment and hope for forgiveness from his Creator through the vastness of His mercy, then there has been an increase in the punishment of evils, indeed (1) the punishment of one evil, and a decrease in the reward of good deeds, and in that there is a breach of promise (khulf fi al-wa'd). For He, exalted, promised that He would reward a good deed tenfold and an evil deed with its like, indeed He promised seven hundredfold by His saying: 'The likeness of those who spend their wealth in the way of Allah is like a seed that grows seven spikes, in each spike a hundred grains' (2). Then He was not content with that, but promised multiplied multiples by His saying: 'And Allah multiplies for whom He wills' (3), and His saying: 'Who is it that will lend Allah a good loan, so that He may multiply it for them many times?' (4). And they (the Mu'tazila) do not allow breach of promise in threat (al-wa'id), so how do they allow breach of promise in promise (al-wa'd)? And it is by consensus of the rational people among the signs of blame and it is ugly in itself. So according to their claim, He did not confine Himself in evil deeds to a recompense like them, but rather added to them what has no limit, and He did not grant for a good deed even its like, let alone tenfold and seven hundredfold. This is the breach of promise beyond which there is no breach.
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage is an argument against the Mu'tazila position that a believer who commits a major sin will be eternally punished. Al-Nasafi presents a reductio ad absurdum: If a person comes with the best of deeds, namely faith (tasdiq), and only commits a limited number of sins that do not reach the extreme of obstinate denial, then punishing him eternally would nullify the reward of his good deeds and would constitute a breach of promise (khulf) on God's part. Al-Nasafi first states the premise: the man has performed the best of deeds and many righteous acts, while his sins are few and not extreme. Then he argues that if such a man is eternally punished, that would mean increasing the punishment for sins and decreasing the reward for good deeds, which is a breach of the promise. He supports this by citing Quranic verses that promise multiplication of rewards: a good deed gets at least ten times, and spending in God's way gets seven hundred times, and even more. God promised to multiply for whom He wills. The Mu'tazila do not allow breach of promise in threat (al-wa'id) – i.e., they insist God must punish as He threatened. But here, al-Nasafi points out that they are inconsistent because they would be accepting a breach in the promise of reward (al-wa'd). Breach of promise is universally considered blameworthy and ugly. Therefore, the Mu'tazila position leads to a greater absurdity: on their view, God would be over-punishing sins and under-rewarding good deeds, which is the worst kind of breach. Thus, the argument concludes that it is impossible for God to eternally punish a believer who has faith and good deeds, because that would contradict His promise of multiplied rewards.

Key Terms
التصديق (tasdiq - affirmation of faith)
الخلف (khulf - breach of promise)
الوعد (al-wa'd - promise)
الوعيد (al-wa'id - threat)
الحسنة (hasana - good deed)
السيئة (sayyi'a - evil deed)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God can arbitrarily punish or reward anyone for any sin, but the argument shows that God's promises bind Him to act justly and consistently.
Correction: The passage argues that God's promise of multiplied reward for good deeds makes it impossible for Him to eternally punish a believer who has faith and good deeds, as that would constitute a breach of promise (khulf).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does eternal punishment for a believer with many good deeds entail?
A) It is a fulfillment of God's threat.
B) It constitutes a breach of God's promise (khulf).
C) It shows God's mercy.
D) It is possible if the sin is great.

Key Takeaway
Eternal punishment for a believer contradicts God's promise of multiplied reward, making it impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own sins, do you truly believe that God's promise of multiplied reward for your faith and good deeds outweighs them? Or do you secretly fear that your sins are too many and too great, as if God's mercy is limited by His justice?
?
If you were to judge yourself the way the Mu'tazila judge the sinner, would you condemn yourself to eternal punishment? Or do you hold on to the hope that God's promise of reward is greater than your sins? What does your answer reveal about your understanding of God's attributes?
?
Al-Nasafi argues that it is impossible for God to break His promise of reward. How does this truth change the way you view your daily acts of worship and repentance? Do you live as if God's promise is certain, or do you let fear of punishment overshadow your hope in His mercy?

The Major Sinner Is Not a Polytheist or Hypocrite
The author defines shirk and nifaq, excludes the major sinner, and illustrates with a debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a major sinner is not considered a polytheist or hypocrite according to Maturidi theology, based on definitions of shirk and nifaq and the argument from the story of Yusuf's brothers.

Core Idea
Committing major sins does not make one a polytheist or hypocrite.

This lesson clarifies an important theological distinction: a Muslim who commits major sins remains a believer, not a polytheist or hypocrite. The author defines shirk and nifaq, then uses a compelling example from the story of Prophet Yusuf's brothers to show that even grave sins do not constitute hypocrisy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the author's concise style.
2
Identify the definitions of shirk and nifaq
The passage defines shirk as belief in a partner with Allah, and refers to a previous explanation of nifaq. Spot these definitions.
3
Understand the exclusion of the major sinner
The author explicitly states that the major sinner does not believe in a partner (not a polytheist) and does not fall under the definition of hypocrisy. Why? Because his actions don't change his inner belief.
The key is that nifaq requires inner disbelief, not just sinful acts.
4
Analyze the story of 'Ata' and Hasan
Read how 'Ata' used the example of Yusuf's brothers to argue that major sins do not make one a hypocrite. See how Hasan conceded.
The brothers lied, broke promises, and betrayed trust—yet remained believers.
5
Note the final qualification about istihlal
The author suggests that the hadith about signs of a hypocrite can be interpreted as referring to one who deems sin permissible (istihlal), not just commits it.
This preserves the hadith while protecting the status of the major sinner.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإيمان وكذا الإشراك اعتقاد شريك الله تعالى إما في العبادة كمشركي العرب، وإما التخليق كالمجوس، وصاحب الكبيرة لا يعتقد ذلك ولا يشرك البتة بعبادة ربه أحدا، وقد بينا أيضا ماهية النفاق وأن صاحب الكبيرة ليس بداخل في حده بل هو على الضد من حال المنافق، وما ذكر من الحديث فذلك علامة المنافق في الأغلب فإما أن تكون هي بنفسها نفاقا فلا، وقد روى أن عطاء لما مذهب الحسن سمع قال: قولوا له إن أخوة يوسف ائتمنوا فخانوا حيث ألقوه في غياهب الجب، وحدثوا فكذبوا بقولهم: أكله الذئب، ووعدوا بقولهم: وإنا له لحافظون فأخلفوا هل صاروا بذلك منافقين؟ فقيل للحسن ذلك، فقال: صدق عطاء ورجع عن ذلك على أن الحديث يمكن حمله على الاستحلال، والله الموفق.
Faith and likewise polytheism: belief in a partner with Allah, either in worship like the polytheists of the Arabs, or in creation like the Magians. The committer of a major sin does not believe that and does not associate anyone at all in worship of his Lord. And we have already explained the essence of hypocrisy and that the committer of a major sin does not fall under its definition; rather, he is opposite to the state of the hypocrite. And what is mentioned of the hadith, that is a sign of the hypocrite in most cases; but that they are themselves hypocrisy? No. And it has been narrated that 'Ata', when he heard the opinion of al-Hasan, said: 'Say to him: The brothers of Yusuf were entrusted and betrayed when they threw him into the darkness of the well, and they spoke and lied by saying "The wolf ate him," and they promised by saying "We will surely be his guardians" and they broke their promise. Did they become hypocrites by that?' So that was said to al-Hasan, and he said: 'Ata' spoke the truth.' And he retracted from that on the basis that the hadith can be interpreted as referring to those who deem (the sin) permissible. And Allah is the one who grants success.
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage begins by affirming that the committer of a major sin (sahib al-kabira) is not a polytheist, because he does not believe in a partner with Allah. It then distinguishes him from the hypocrite (munafiq) by stating that the definition of hypocrisy does not apply to him. The author then narrates a story of 'Ata' arguing with Hasan al-Basri: 'Ata' used the example of the brothers of Yusuf who committed betrayal, lying, and breaking promises, yet they are not considered hypocrites. Hasan conceded this point and retracted his earlier view, with the qualification that the hadith about the signs of a hypocrite can be understood as referring to someone who deems the sin permissible (istihlal), not to the mere commission of it. Thus, the major sinner remains a believer.

Key Terms
الإيمان
الإشراك
الكبيرة
النفاق
الاستحلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Committing major sins makes one a hypocrite (munafiq) because the hadith lists signs like lying and breaking promises.
Correction: The hadith signs are only external indicators in most cases, not the essence of hypocrisy. A person can commit these sins without being a true hypocrite, as demonstrated by the brothers of Yusuf.

Checkpoint

C) They became disbelievers.
D) Their status is unknown.

Key Takeaway
A major sinner remains a believer and is not a polytheist or hypocrite; only those who deem sins permissible or hold false beliefs fall into those categories.

The Major Sinner Remains a Believer Despite Sin
The author argues that the major sinner remains a believer and addresses counterarguments from Quranic verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why, according to the author, committing a major sin does not remove one from the fold of faith, and how verses threatening punishment are reconciled with this position.

Core Idea
A believer remains a believer despite major sin because faith is affirmation, not infallibility.

In this passage, the author defends the position that a person who commits a major sin is still a believer. He builds on the earlier definition of faith as tasdiq and addresses common objections from Quranic verses that seem to threaten eternal punishment. Let's see how he argues that such verses do not undermine his conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to understand the flow of argument.
Notice the author's concise style and logical connections.
2
Identify the main claim
Find the sentence where the author states that the major sinner remains a believer. What is the basis for this claim?
Look for the phrase 'كان مؤمنا' and the reason given.
3
Examine the handling of negative names
The author lists names like fasiq and explains them. Why are these names not contradictory to faith?
Think about the difference between a name for an action and a name for the person's essence.
4
Analyze the response to Quranic verses
The author gives two interpretations for verses that threaten punishment. Identify both possibilities.
One relates to those who deem sins lawful, the other to Allah's will.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how the two parts of the passage work together to support the conclusion. How does the author safeguard the believer's status?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن هذا الرجل كان مؤمنا لما معه من التصديق وبالكبيرة ما انعدم، وما حدث له من الأسماء الخبيثة كالفاسق والظالم والفاجر وغيرها، فتلك أسماء يستحقها بأفعال ليست بمنافية للإيمان فثبوتها لا يكون منافيا لكونه مؤمنا، ثم لما لم يصر كافرا لانعدام التكذيب لم لم يبق مؤمنا ومعه التصديق ولم يوجد منه ما يضاده وقد خرج الجواب عن تعلقهم بقوله تعالى: الذين يأكلون أموال اليتامى ظلما وبغيره من الآيات التي ليس فيها ذكر الخلود أن ذلك يجوز أن تكون واردة في المستحلين، ويجوز أن تكون واردة في غير المستحلين، ويكون ذلك معلقا بمشيئة الله تعالى على ما قررنا.
Then indeed this man was a believer because he possessed affirmation (tasdiq), and by the major sin he did not become devoid (of faith). And what occurred to him of evil names like fasiq, zalim, fajir, and others, these are names he deserves for actions that are not contradictory to faith; so their establishment does not contradict his being a believer. Then, since he did not become a disbeliever due to the absence of denial (takdhib), why would he not remain a believer while he has affirmation and nothing opposed to it has occurred from him? And the answer to their clinging to His statement: 'Those who consume the wealth of orphans unjustly' and other verses that do not mention eternity (khulud) is that it is possible that they refer to those who deem it permissible (mustahillun), and it is possible that they refer to other than those who deem it permissible, and that is contingent upon the will of Allah as we have established.
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage continues the argument. The author asserts that the major sinner remains a believer because he still possesses tasdiq (affirmation of faith). The negative names like fasiq are earned for specific acts but do not negate the underlying faith. The author then addresses the objection based on verses that threaten punishment for sins, such as the verse about consuming orphans' wealth. He argues that these verses can be interpreted in two ways: either they refer to those who consider such actions lawful (istihlal) or they refer to others, and the actual punishment is subject to Allah's will. Thus, they do not prove that the major sinner will be eternally punished.

Key Terms
التصديق
التكذيب
الفاسق
المستحل
الخلود
المشيئة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that being called 'fasiq' in the Quran indicates complete loss of faith.
Correction: The author explains it is a name for an action, not a negation of faith.

Checkpoint

A) Because the believer is always forgiven.
B) Because the verses may refer only to those who deem the sin permissible.
C) Because the believer never sins.
D) Because the punishment is only for hypocrites.

Key Takeaway
The major sinner remains a believer because faith (tasdiq) is not negated by sinful actions; negative labels are for deeds, not belief, and punishment verses may refer to those who deem sins lawful or are subject to Allah's will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Have you allowed shame over a sin to make you question your iman? What would change if you truly internalized that your affirmation of Allah remains unless you actively deny Him?
?
The passage warns that deeming sins permissible (istihlal) can remove faith. Is there any sin in your life that you have normalized or justified to the point that you no longer feel its weight before Allah? How can you distinguish between the weakness of falling into sin and the danger of making lawful what Allah has forbidden?
?
The author says negative labels like 'fasiq' describe actions, not your essence as a believer. When someone calls you a bad name because of a sin, do you let that define you? Or do you hold fast to the truth that you are still a believer? How does this lesson empower you to respond to both your own failures and the judgments of others?

Absolute Fasiq Is the Disbeliever
Author defines absolute vs obedient fasiq and refutes Mu'tazilite view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between an absolute fasiq (disbeliever) and an obedient fasiq (Muslim sinner), and why the Quranic verse refers to the disbeliever.

Core Idea
An absolute fasiq has no obedience; a Muslim sinner still has faith.

This lesson clarifies a key doctrinal point: when the Quran calls someone 'fasiq', it does not necessarily mean a Muslim sinner. The author shows that the term 'absolute fasiq' applies to the disbeliever alone, using both definition and context. This refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that a Muslim who commits a major sin becomes an absolute fasiq.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the author's argument.
Notice the author's distinct definitions of absolute and obedient fasiq.
2
Identify the two types
Find where the author defines absolute fasiq (فاسق مطلق) and obedient fasiq (فاسق مطيع). Understand the criteria: absolute fasiq has no obedience at all.
3
Examine the evidence
Look at the evidence from the Quranic context (سياق الآية). The following verse (Sajdah 32:20) mentions punishment of the Fire, which applies to disbelievers, not Muslim sinners.
This context is key to refuting the Mu'tazilite claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[هناك فاسق مطلق وفاسق مطيع] وتعلقهم بقوله تعالى: أَفَمَن كَانَ مُؤْمِنًا كَمَن كَانَ فَاسِقًا لَا يَسْتَوُونَ قلنا : الآية وردت في الفاسق المطلق، والمؤمن ليس بفاسق مطلق بل هو فاسق بما ارتكب من المعصية مطيع بما معه من الإيمان والطاعات والفاسق المطلق من فاسقا من جميع الوجوه بحيث ليست معه طاعة ولا هو موصوف بها بوجه، فأما من كان مطيعا بما هو رأس كل طاعة وبما لا يحصى من الطاعات فليس هو بفاسق مطلق، وقد وقع في أفواه من لا علم لهم بهذا الباب أن الفاسق المطلق هو مرتكب الكبيرة، ولكن ذلك باطل وقع فيما بين الناس بتلقين المعتزلة. والدليل على أن المراد بالفاسق المطلق الكافر سياق الآية وهو قوله تعالى: وَقِيلَ لَهُمْ ذُوقُوا عَذَابَ النَّارِ الَّذِي كُنتُم بِهِ تُكَذِبُونَ وهذا وصف الكفار دون صاحب الكبيرة. التكلم بلفظ العموم والمراد به الخصوص سائغ عند أهل اللغة وما يزعمون أن الإخبار بتعذيب مرتكبي الذنوب وردت مطلقة عامة ولو أخرج منها شيء كان خلفا، وذلك كذب إذ الكذب هو الإخبار عن المخبر لا على ما هو به والكذب على الله تعالى محال ولا وجه إلى القول بتخصيص من عفى عنه وغفر له في الآية، لأن صيغة العموم متى وردت متعرية عن دليل الخصوص كانت دلالة على إرادة المتكلم جميع أفراد ما يتناوله اللفظ كأنه نص على كل فرد
[There is an absolute fasiq and an obedient fasiq.] And their clinging to His statement: 'Is he who is a believer like he who is a fasiq? They are not equal.' We say: The verse was revealed about the absolute fasiq, and the believer is not an absolute fasiq; rather, he is a fasiq due to the sin he committed, obedient due to what he possesses of faith and acts of obedience. And the absolute fasiq is one who is fasiq from all aspects, such that there is no obedience with him and he is not described with it in any way. As for one who is obedient in that which is the head of every obedience and with countless acts of obedience, he is not an absolute fasiq. And it has occurred in the mouths of those who have no knowledge of this subject that the absolute fasiq is the committer of a major sin, but that is false, having spread among people by the instigation of the Mu'tazila. The evidence that what is meant by the absolute fasiq is the disbeliever is the context of the verse: 'And it will be said to them, "Taste the punishment of the Fire which you used to deny."' And this is a description of the disbelievers, not of the committer of a major sin. Speaking in general terms with the intent of specific meaning is permissible according to the people of language. And what they claim that the reports of punishment for the committers of sins came in absolute general terms, and if anything were excluded from them it would be a lie, that is a lie because lying is informing about something contrary to what it is, and lying about Allah is impossible. There is no way to say that those pardoned and forgiven are specified in the verse, because when a general expression appears without an indicator of specification, it indicates the speaker's intention of all individuals included in the word, as if it were a text for each individual.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage deals with two interrelated arguments. First, the author refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that the term 'fasiq' in the Qur'an refers to the committer of a major sin, and that such a person is absolutely fasiq. He distinguishes between absolute fasiq (who has no obedience at all) and a believer who commits sins but also has obedience (iman). The context of the verse (Sajdah 32:20) shows that the absolute fasiq is the disbeliever, not the Muslim sinner. Second, the author addresses the objection that general threat verses apply to all sinners, and excluding anyone would be a lie. He argues that general terms can be intended for specific meanings (al-amm al-murad bihi al-khass), which is a valid linguistic principle. Therefore, the threat verses may be referring to disbelievers or certain categories, and it is not a lie because the speaker's intention is what matters.

Key Terms
فاسق مطلق
فاسق مطيع
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A Muslim who commits a major sin becomes an absolute fasiq (disbeliever).
Correction: A Muslim sinner still has faith and obedience, so he is not absolute fasiq; only a disbeliever with no obedience is absolute fasiq.

Checkpoint
What does the author cite as evidence that 'absolute fasiq' in the verse refers to a disbeliever?
A) A hadith
B) The context of the verse mentioning punishment of the Fire
C) Consensus of scholars
D) The definition of fasiq

Key Takeaway
An absolute fasiq is a disbeliever with no obedience, while a Muslim sinner remains obedient through faith.

General Terms with Specific Intent
Author defends linguistic principle that general terms can be intended for specific meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the principle that general expressions in the Quran can be intended for specific referents, and why this does not constitute lying.

Core Idea
General terms can be specific; it's not a lie.

The Mu'tazila argued that threat verses in the Quran apply to all sinners, and excluding anyone would be a lie. The author counters by explaining a well-known linguistic principle: general terms may be intended to refer to specific individuals. This lesson unpacks that argument and its basis in Arabic language.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its translation. Pay attention to the key terms.
The passage discusses a linguistic principle and a refutation.
2
Understand the principle
The author states that speaking in general terms with specific intent (العموم والمراد به الخصوص) is permissible in Arabic. This means a general word can refer to a specific subset.
3
Refutation of the lying objection
The Mu'tazila claimed that if any sinner is excluded from threat verses, it would be a lie. The author defines lying as informing contrary to reality, which is impossible for Allah. Also, a general term without specification indicates intent for all individuals.
This shows the importance of context and linguistic rules.

The Waqf Accusation and Al-Maturidi's Doctrine of 'Umum
Al-Nasafi rebuts the waqfi accusation and clarifies al-Maturidi's doctrine on 'umum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nature of the waqfi accusation against Al-Maturidi and his actual doctrine that acting upon 'umum is wajib, not farḍ.

Core Idea
Al-Maturidi was not waqifi; he affirmed 'umum as wajib based on apparent proof.

In this passage, Al-Nasafi confronts the claim that Abu Mansur al-Maturidi was a 'waqifi' on the issue of 'umum (generality). He shows that this accusation stems from ignorance of al-Maturidi's actual position. The key is understanding the distinction between farḍ (definitive) and wajib (probable). Let's read the passage and see how al-Nasafi clarifies the matter.

Guided Path
1
Read the accusation
Notice how al-Nasafi criticizes an unnamed author for making a claim without considering its implications. He then observes that later scholars blindly followed and mislabeled al-Maturidi.
The accusation is that al-Maturidi was waqifi on 'umum.
2
Identify al-Maturidi's doctrine
Find where al-Nasafi states that al-Maturidi's actual doctrine is that acting upon 'umum and the absolute form is wajib (obligatory).
Wajib is defined as what is established by non-definitive proof.
3
Understand the farḍ/wajib distinction
Note the contrast: farḍ is from definitive proof, wajib is from apparent proof. This distinction is crucial to understand why al-Maturidi is not waqifi.
Al-Maturidi held a positive position (wajib), not suspension.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد أطلق في كتابه أن الفاسق المطلق هو صاحب الكبيرة ولست أدري أقال ذلك علمًا منه بما يؤدي إليه هذا القول أم سنح له شيء فتكلم به من غير العلم بما يؤدي إليه والسرائر موكولة إلى الله تعالى وهو أعلم بالعقائد والضمائر، فاتبعه جميع المنتسبين إلى التحقيق في ذلك واقتدوا به وما اشتغل أحد منهم أنه بأي طريق يتكلم في مسألة الوعيد، وسموا الشيخ أبا منصور رحمه الله الواقفي في هذه المسألة جهلاً منهم بمذهبه إذ حقيقة مذهبه أن العمل بالعموم وبمطلق الصيغة واجب، إذ الواجب هو ما ثبت بدليل غير مقطوع به وهو دليل الظاهر، فأما ما ثبت بدليل مقطوع به فهو الفرض ، ولم يرد عن أحد من متقدمي أصحابنا أنهم قالوا مطلق الأمر للوجوب والقول بالعموم واجب وهو ما ذهب إليه الشيخ أبو منصور، الواقفي فهو من يقف في ذلك عملا واعتقادا، ثم إن من ساعد هذا القائل بالعموم وظهر منه القول بجواز مغفرة صاحب الكبيرة يضيق عليه الأمر بطريق التخصيص كما ذهب إليه الشيخ أبو منصور، وجميع من ساعدنا في هذه المسألة من فحول المتكلمين كأبي الحسن الأشعري و من تابعه وابن الراوندي
And he stated in his book that the absolute sinner (fasiq mutlaq) is the one who commits a major sin (kabira). I do not know if he said that from knowledge of what this saying leads to, or if something occurred to him so he spoke without knowing its implications; hearts are entrusted to Allah Most High, and He knows best beliefs and consciences. Then all those who claim investigation followed him in that and emulated him, and none of them occupied themselves with how to speak on the issue of threat (wa'id). They called Shaykh Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) 'al-waqifi' in this issue out of ignorance of his doctrine. For the reality of his doctrine is that acting upon generality and upon the absolute form is obligatory (wajib), for what is obligatory is that which is established by a non-definitive proof, i.e., the apparent proof. As for what is established by a definitive proof, it is farḍ. And it has not been reported from any of our early companions that they said: 'The absolute command is for obligation and the saying of generality is obligatory.' This is what Shaykh Abu Mansur held. 'Al-waqifi' is one who stops in that, both in action and belief. Then, whoever assists this speaker of generality and from whom appears the saying of the permissibility of forgiveness for the one who commits a major sin, the matter becomes tight for him by way of specification (takhsis) as Shaykh Abu Mansur held. And all who assisted us in this issue among the leading mutakallimun, such as Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and his followers and Ibn al-Rawandi.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage is a rebuttal of the accusation that Abu Mansur al-Maturidi was a 'waqifi' (one who suspends judgment) on the issue of 'umum. Al-Nasafi criticizes an unnamed author who, in his book, declared that the absolute sinner is the one who commits a major sin. Al-Nasafi expresses uncertainty about whether the author said this knowingly or ignorantly. He then notes that many later scholars followed this author blindly without engaging with the wa'id issue, and they ignorantly labeled al-Maturidi as 'waqifi'. Al-Nasafi clarifies al-Maturidi's actual doctrine: acting upon 'umum and upon the absolute form is wajib (obligatory, in the technical sense of being required but not definitively proven). Here, 'wajib' is contrasted with 'farḍ': farḍ is what is proven by a definitive proof; wajib is what is proven by a non-definitive, apparent proof. Al-Maturidi applied 'umum as a wajib ruling, not farḍ, and this is distinct from the position of those who 'stop' (waqf) in both action and belief. The passage further argues that those who affirm 'umum yet also assert the possibility of forgiveness for major sinners face a contradiction unless they resort to specification (takhsis), which is al-Maturidi's approach. Finally, al-Nasafi lists those who agree with al-Maturidi on this issue: Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, his followers, and Ibn al-Rawandi.

Key Terms
الوقف
الواجب
الفرض
العموم
الوعيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Maturidi suspended judgment on 'umum (was waqifi).
Correction: Al-Maturidi affirmed 'umum as wajib, based on non-definitive proof; he did not suspend judgment.

Checkpoint
Why did al-Nasafi defend al-Maturidi against the waqfi accusation?
A) Al-Maturidi rejected 'umum entirely
B) Al-Maturidi held 'umum as farḍ
C) Al-Maturidi held 'umum as wajib, not farḍ
D) Al-Maturidi was a waqifi in belief

Key Takeaway
Al-Maturidi's position on 'umum is that it is wajib, not farḍ, and this distinguishes him from being waqifi.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a theological disagreement, are you quick to label others (like the scholars who called al-Maturidi waqifi) without fully understanding their position? How can you investigate before judging this week?
?
The passage shows some scholars blindly followed without engaging with the source. Where in your own religious learning do you follow authority without verifying from primary texts? What specific step will you take to change that?
?
Al-Maturidi distinguished between what is wajib (based on apparent proof) and what is farḍ (definitive). Do you treat all religious obligations with the same level of certainty? How might recognizing this distinction affect the sincerity and care with which you practice your faith?

The Implications of 'Umum for Wa'id and Agreement of Scholars
Al-Nasafi explains the need for takhsis and lists supporters of al-Maturidi's view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Maturidi's doctrine of 'umum as wajib relates to the issue of wa'id and the permissibility of forgiveness for major sinners, and which scholars agreed with him.

Core Idea
Affirming 'umum requires accepting takhsis to reconcile with divine forgiveness.

After establishing al-Maturidi's position on 'umum, al-Nasafi addresses a practical implication: if one affirms 'umum and also holds that major sinners may be forgiven, a tension arises. The solution is specification (takhsis). Al-Nasafi also lists other notable scholars who shared al-Maturidi's approach. Let's read the conclusion of his argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
Notice the tension between affirming 'umum (general threat) and allowing forgiveness for major sinners.
The general threat (wa'id) seems to include all sinners unconditionally.
2
Understand the solution
Al-Nasafi states that the matter becomes tight unless one uses takhsis (specification) to limit the generality.
Takhsis is al-Maturidi's approach.
3
Note the supporters
Look at the list of scholars who agreed with al-Maturidi: Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, his followers, and Ibn al-Rawandi.
This shows the view was not isolated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد أطلق في كتابه أن الفاسق المطلق هو صاحب الكبيرة ولست أدري أقال ذلك علمًا منه بما يؤدي إليه هذا القول أم سنح له شيء فتكلم به من غير العلم بما يؤدي إليه والسرائر موكولة إلى الله تعالى وهو أعلم بالعقائد والضمائر، فاتبعه جميع المنتسبين إلى التحقيق في ذلك واقتدوا به وما اشتغل أحد منهم أنه بأي طريق يتكلم في مسألة الوعيد، وسموا الشيخ أبا منصور رحمه الله الواقفي في هذه المسألة جهلاً منهم بمذهبه إذ حقيقة مذهبه أن العمل بالعموم وبمطلق الصيغة واجب، إذ الواجب هو ما ثبت بدليل غير مقطوع به وهو دليل الظاهر، فأما ما ثبت بدليل مقطوع به فهو الفرض ، ولم يرد عن أحد من متقدمي أصحابنا أنهم قالوا مطلق الأمر للوجوب والقول بالعموم واجب وهو ما ذهب إليه الشيخ أبو منصور، الواقفي فهو من يقف في ذلك عملا واعتقادا، ثم إن من ساعد هذا القائل بالعموم وظهر منه القول بجواز مغفرة صاحب الكبيرة يضيق عليه الأمر بطريق التخصيص كما ذهب إليه الشيخ أبو منصور، وجميع من ساعدنا في هذه المسألة من فحول المتكلمين كأبي الحسن الأشعري و من تابعه وابن الراوندي
And he stated in his book that the absolute sinner (fasiq mutlaq) is the one who commits a major sin (kabira). I do not know if he said that from knowledge of what this saying leads to, or if something occurred to him so he spoke without knowing its implications; hearts are entrusted to Allah Most High, and He knows best beliefs and consciences. Then all those who claim investigation followed him in that and emulated him, and none of them occupied themselves with how to speak on the issue of threat (wa'id). They called Shaykh Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) 'al-waqifi' in this issue out of ignorance of his doctrine. For the reality of his doctrine is that acting upon generality and upon the absolute form is obligatory (wajib), for what is obligatory is that which is established by a non-definitive proof, i.e., the apparent proof. As for what is established by a definitive proof, it is farḍ. And it has not been reported from any of our early companions that they said: 'The absolute command is for obligation and the saying of generality is obligatory.' This is what Shaykh Abu Mansur held. 'Al-waqifi' is one who stops in that, both in action and belief. Then, whoever assists this speaker of generality and from whom appears the saying of the permissibility of forgiveness for the one who commits a major sin, the matter becomes tight for him by way of specification (takhsis) as Shaykh Abu Mansur held. And all who assisted us in this issue among the leading mutakallimun, such as Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and his followers and Ibn al-Rawandi.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage is a rebuttal of the accusation that Abu Mansur al-Maturidi was a 'waqifi' (one who suspends judgment) on the issue of 'umum. Al-Nasafi criticizes an unnamed author who, in his book, declared that the absolute sinner is the one who commits a major sin. Al-Nasafi expresses uncertainty about whether the author said this knowingly or ignorantly. He then notes that many later scholars followed this author blindly without engaging with the wa'id issue, and they ignorantly labeled al-Maturidi as 'waqifi'. Al-Nasafi clarifies al-Maturidi's actual doctrine: acting upon 'umum and upon the absolute form is wajib (obligatory, in the technical sense of being required but not definitively proven). Here, 'wajib' is contrasted with 'farḍ': farḍ is what is proven by a definitive proof; wajib is what is proven by a non-definitive, apparent proof. Al-Maturidi applied 'umum as a wajib ruling, not farḍ, and this is distinct from the position of those who 'stop' (waqf) in both action and belief. The passage further argues that those who affirm 'umum yet also assert the possibility of forgiveness for major sinners face a contradiction unless they resort to specification (takhsis), which is al-Maturidi's approach. Finally, al-Nasafi lists those who agree with al-Maturidi on this issue: Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, his followers, and Ibn al-Rawandi.

Key Terms
التخصيص
الوعيد
المغفرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Maturidi's view on 'umum was unique and rejected by other scholars.
Correction: Many leading scholars, including al-Ash'ari and Ibn al-Rawandi, agreed with al-Maturidi's approach of takhsis.

Checkpoint
What does Al-Nasafi present as the solution to the tension between affirming 'umum and allowing forgiveness for major sinners?
A) Abandoning the doctrine of 'umum
B) Takhsis (specification)
C) Denying forgiveness
D) Labeling opponents as waqifi

Key Takeaway
Al-Maturidi's solution of takhsis for the wa'id issue was shared by prominent scholars, showing it was not an isolated view.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God can forgive your worst sin, or do you assume that some sins are beyond His mercy? How does the principle of takhsis challenge your hidden doubt?
?
Al-Maturidi and al-Ash'ari, two pillars of Sunni theology, agreed on takhsis. If you find yourself leaning toward a stricter view, are you willing to submit to their collective wisdom? What pride keeps you from doing so?
?
If you knew that God's threat is specified only for those who die unrepentant, would that make you less afraid of sinning? Or would it drive you deeper into repentance? Examine your heart: which response is stronger?

The Necessity of Distinguishing Kufr from Lesser Sins in the Afterlife
Argues that divine wisdom necessitates a distinction between kufr and lesser sins in the afterlife.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi scholars argue that divine wisdom necessitates a distinction between disbelief and lesser sins regarding forgiveness and eternal punishment in the afterlife.

Core Idea
Divine justice requires that kufr and other sins be treated differently in the afterlife.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi's Tabsirat al-Adilla addresses a key point of disagreement between theological schools. Some hold that even disbelievers may be forgiven, while Maturidis argue that God's wisdom demands a clear distinction between the righteous and the wicked. Since this distinction is not fully realized in this world, it must be manifested in the next. Read on to see how the author supports this view with Quranic verses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Notice that the passage begins by stating that for those who follow the People of Hadith (allowing forgiveness even for disbelievers), no distinction is needed. But for Maturidi scholars who insist on divine wisdom requiring a distinction between wrongdoer and doer of good, a distinction is essential.
2
Examine the Quranic evidence
The author cites three verses that condemn equating the righteous with the wicked. Read each verse and consider how it supports the need for differential treatment.
The verses are: 38:28, 45:21, and 68:35-36.
3
Follow the logical inference
Since the world does not fully differentiate between the two groups (both experience good and bad), the full distinction must be realized in the afterlife. This necessitates a difference between kufr and lesser sins.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The conclusion is that forgiveness is possible for sins less than kufr, but not for kufr itself; eternal punishment is specific to kufr, while lesser sins may lead to temporary punishment or forgiveness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لا حاجة إلى الفرق بين الكفر وغيره من الذنوب في حق جواز العفو والتعذيب والتخليد في النار وغير التخليد لمن يرى رأي متكلمي أهل الحديث في جواز العفو عن الكافر. فأما من يقول من أصحابنا أن لا بد في الحكمة من التفرقة بين المسيء والمحسن على ما استبعد الله تعالى من التسوية بينهما بقوله: أَمْ نَجْعَلُ الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا وَعَمِلُوا الصَّالِحَاتِ كَالْمُفْسِدِينَ فِي الْأَرْضِ أَمْ نَجْعَلُ الْمُتَّقِينَ كَالْفُجَّارِ وقوله: أَمْ حَسِبَ الَّذِينَ اجْتَرَحُوا السَّيِّئَاتِ أَنْ نَجْعَلَهُمْ كَالَّذِينَ آمَنُوا وَعَمِلُوا الصَّالِحَاتِ سَوَاءً مَحْيَاهُمْ وَمَمَاتُهُمْ سَاءَ مَا يَحْكُمُونَ وقوله تعالى: أَفَنَجْعَلُ الْمُسْلِمِينَ كَالْمُجْرِمِينَ مَا لَكُمْ كَيْفَ تَحْكُمُونَ ثم لا تفرقة بين الفريقين في الدنيا فلابد من أن يفرق بينهما في الآخرة فهؤلاء يحتاجون إلى الفرق بين الكفر وبين ما دونه من الذنوب في جواز العفو عما دون الكفر وامتناعه فيه، وثبوت تخليد العذاب فيه، وسقوطه عما دونه.
Then there is no need to distinguish between disbelief and other sins regarding the permissibility of forgiveness and punishment and eternal punishment in the Fire and non-eternity for one who holds the opinion of the speculative theologians of the People of Hadith on the permissibility of forgiveness for the disbeliever. As for those of our companions who say that there must in wisdom be a distinction between the wrongdoer and the doer of good, as God, exalted, deemed unlikely the equality between them by His saying: 'Or shall We make those who believe and do righteous deeds like the corrupters in the earth, or shall We make the righteous like the wicked?' and His saying: 'Or do those who commit evil deeds think that We will make them like those who believe and do righteous deeds, equal in their life and their death? Evil is that which they judge.' and His saying: 'Should We make the Muslims like the criminals? What is the matter with you? How do you judge?' Then there is no distinction between the two groups in the world, so there must be a distinction between them in the Hereafter. These need to distinguish between disbelief and what is less than it of sins regarding the permissibility of forgiveness for what is less than disbelief and its impossibility in it, and the affirmation of eternal punishment in it, and its removal from what is less than it.
Source pages p. 411

Commentary
This passage discusses the necessity of distinguishing between kufr (disbelief) and other sins in matters of forgiveness and punishment. The author notes that for those who follow the speculative theologians of the People of Hadith (who allow forgiveness even for disbelievers), no such distinction is needed. However, for Maturidi scholars who hold that divine wisdom (al-ḥikma) requires differentiating between the wrongdoer and the doer of good, the distinction is essential. The author cites three Quranic verses that condemn equating the righteous and the wicked, implying that God must treat them differently. Since this difference is not fully realized in the worldly life, it must be manifested in the afterlife. Therefore, these scholars need to differentiate between kufr and lesser sins: forgiveness is possible for sins less than kufr but not for kufr itself; eternal punishment is specific to kufr, while lesser sins may lead to temporary punishment or forgiveness.

Misconception: All sins are equal in terms of forgiveness and punishment in the afterlife.
Correction: This passage shows that kufr (disbelief) is distinct: forgiveness is not possible for kufr, but it is for lesser sins; eternal punishment is specific to kufr.

C) Because the People of Hadith require it.
D) Because kufr is the only unforgivable sin without any exception.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi theology holds that divine wisdom requires a clear distinction between kufr and lesser sins in the afterlife: kufr is the only sin that merits eternal punishment and is not subject to forgiveness.

?

?

?
The Quranic verses cited condemn treating the righteous and wicked as equal. In your daily life, do you strive to distinguish yourself from those who persist in sin, or do you find yourself complacent, assuming you're already on the right side? What one change can you make today to align your actions with God's wisdom?
